The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Condition of Leaving Fruits: Custom vs. Strict Qiyas
Dispute between Sheikhayn and Muhammad on condition of leaving fruit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of the Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad regarding the condition of leaving fruits, identify the role of custom (urf) in Hanafi istihsan, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Imam Muhammad's istihsan permits a customary condition of leaving fruits, overriding the strict qiyas of gharar.

This lesson examines an internal Hanafi disagreement: does a condition to leave fruit on the tree after sale invalidate the contract? The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold it void due to uncertainty, while Imam Muhammad allows it for mature trees based on custom (urf). We will explore the reasoning, the tarjih, and how the fatwa evolved to favor Muhammad's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is a sale of fruit with an explicit condition that the buyer leaves the fruit on the tree after purchase. The ruling is disputed: void (fasid) according to the Shaykhayn, valid (sahih) according to Imam Muhammad for mature trees.
2
State the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: sale is void unconditionally because the fruit that grows later is unknown (majhul) and remains guaranteed in the seller's property, creating gharar. Imam Muhammad: sale is valid for fully grown trees (mutanahiyah) by istihsan, because the condition is customary and thus removes uncertainty.
The zahir al-riwayah position is that of the Shaykhayn; Muhammad's view is a nawadir/istihsan.
3
Analyse the proofs and method
Sheikhayn use strict qiyas: the later fruit is unknown and guaranteed in the seller's property, analogous to selling unknown property. Muhammad uses istihsan based on custom (urf): people commonly allow such conditions, so the condition does not cause harmful uncertainty. This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) where custom specifies the effective cause.
The argument type is qiyas khafi — the illa (gharar) is negated by customary practice.
4
Evaluate the tarjih and fatwa
Al-Bahr and al-Asrar report the fatwa is on Muhammad's view; al-Tahawi adopted it. Al-Muntaqa states Abu Yusuf later joined Muhammad. However, al-Tuhfah refines: the correct position is that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because the actual custom was not a contractual condition but informal permission. Thus the fatwa upholds validity for mature trees, but the stronger basis is the informal custom.
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view, but the practice was informal permission without condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكم فساد البيع عند الشيخين سواء تناهى عظمها أو لا؛ لأن ما زاد وحدث من الترك في ملك البائع مضمون عند البيع، وهو مجهول، وقال محمد: لا يفسد في المتناهية مستحسنا؛ لأنه شرط متعارف، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة. وفي «البحر» نقلا عن «الأسرار»: الفتوى على قول محمد، وبه أخذ الطحاوي، وفي «المنتقى»: ضم إليه أبو يوسف، وفي «التحفة»: والصحيح قولهما؛ لأن التعامل لم يكن بشرط الترك، وإنما كان بالإذن بالترك من غير شرط. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر».
And the ruling of the invalidity of the sale according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is the same whether the tree has reached its full size or not, because what increases and occurs from leaving on the seller's property is guaranteed (maḍmūn) in the sale, and it is unknown (majhūl). And Muhammad said: it is not invalid in the case of a mature tree, as an istiḥsān, because it is a customary condition, and it is the saying of the three imams (i.e., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad? No, the phrase 'الأئمة الثلاثة' here refers to the three Caliphs or earlier scholars). In al-Baḥr, quoting al-Asrār: the fatwa is on Muhammad's statement, and al-Ṭaḥāwī adopted it. In al-Muntaqā: Abu Yusuf joined him. In al-Tuḥfah: the correct is the statement of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) because the practice was not with the condition of leaving, but rather with permission to leave without a condition. Thus in Majmaʿ al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a condition to leave fruit on the tree after sale. The base ruling (from the matn) is that such a condition invalidates the sale. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that the sale is void regardless of whether the tree has reached full maturity (متناهية). Their reasoning is that the fruit that grows after the sale remains the seller's property and is guaranteed (مضمون) in the contract, but its quantity is unknown (مجهول), leading to gharar. Imam Muhammad, however, makes an exception for mature trees (المتناهية) based on istihsan (juristic preference): the condition is customary (شرط متعارف) and therefore does not invalidate the sale. He attributes his view to the three imams (possibly earlier scholars). The text then notes the fatwa: according to al-Bahr and al-Asrar, the fatwa is on Muhammad's view, and al-Tahawi adopted it. Al-Muntaqa reports that Abu Yusuf joined Muhammad in this view. However, in al-Tuhfah, the correct position is stated to be that of the two (i.e., the Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because the actual custom was not a contractual condition but merely informal permission to leave the fruit. This refines the dispute: while Sheikhayn formally say the sale is void, the operative practice (تعامل) was that people gave permission without a stipulation, and thus the fatwa moves towards permissibility.

Key Terms
المتناهية (al-mutanāhiyah): Trees that have reached their full size and are mature; Muhammad excludes them from the ruling of invalidity.
شرط متعارف (sharṭ mutaʿāraf): A condition that is customary and commonly practiced among people; considered valid by Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For which type of tree did Imam Muhammad make an exception to the rule that a condition of leaving invalidates the sale?
A) Trees that are not yet mature.
B) Trees that have reached full maturity (متناهية).
C) Trees that bear fruit twice a year.
D) Trees that are owned by the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa upholds Imam Muhammad's istihsan for mature trees, but the formal position of the Shaykhayn remains in the zahir al-riwayah; the actual custom was informal permission.

Exception of Known Quantity in Fruit Sale
Ruling on excluding a known quantity from a fruit sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on excepting a known quantity of fruit from a sale and explain why a single ratl exception is valid by consensus.

Core Idea
A known exception of a small quantity from a fruit sale is valid; exceptions that risk equaling the whole are invalid.

In Hanafi sales law, the buyer and seller can agree to exclude a certain portion of the commodity from the sale. This is called istithna. But to avoid uncertainty (gharar), the excepted quantity must be precisely known and not so large that it could be the entire asset. This lesson examines the specific ruling for fruit sales, focusing on the classical text's treatment of ratl measurements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act under consideration is 'istithna qadr ma'lum' – excluding a known quantity from the object of sale in a fruit transaction.
2
Determine the ruling
The ruling is that such an exception is valid (sahih) provided the quantity is known. The texts of al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, and al-Quduri state that the exception must be in known ratls.
3
Understand the condition
The condition for validity is that the excepted quantity must be known with precision. If unknown, the sale is void due to gharar.
4
Distinguish single vs multiple ratls
Excepting a single ratl is permissible by consensus because it is a small portion from a large whole. Multiple ratls might risk that the excepted quantity equals the entire produce, which would be an invalid exception of the whole from the whole.
This distinction is based on the ratio of exception to total.
5
Apply the underlying reasoning (talil)
The reasoning is 'istithna al-qalil min al-kathir' – exception of a little from much is safe and does not cause uncertainty. The prohibition of multiple ratls is to avoid the possibility of 'istithna al-kull min al-kull' (exception of the whole from the whole).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كاستثناء قدر معلوم منها. قال صاحب «الكنز» و«التنوير» و«القُدُوري»: أرطالا معلومة، وذكر بعض شراح «الهداية»: إن المستثنى لو كان رطلا واحدا يجوز؛ لأنه استثنى القليل من الكثير بخلاف الأرطال بجواز أن لا يكون إلا ذلك القدر، فيكون استثناء الكل من الكل. وقال في «المنح»: «وقيد بالأرطال؛ لأنه لو استثنى رطلا واحدا جاز اتفاقا». انتهى.
Like the exception of a known quantity from it. The author of al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, and al-Quduri said: 'known ratls'. And some commentators of al-Hidayah mentioned: if the excepted amount were a single ratl, it is permissible, because he excepted a little from much, unlike ratls due to the possibility that it might not be exactly that amount, resulting in an exception of the whole from the whole. And in al-Minaḥ: 'It is restricted to ratls because if he excepted a single ratl, it is permissible by consensus.' End.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on استثناء (exception) when selling fruits. The basic rule is that if a seller says 'I sell you this fruit except for a certain known quantity,' that exception is valid only if the quantity is known with precision. The texts of al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, and al-Quduri specify that the exception must be in known ratls (a unit of weight). However, some commentators on al-Hidayah explain that if the exception is for a single ratl, it is permissible because it is a small portion from a large whole; but when the exception is for multiple ratls, there is risk that the excepted amount equals the entire produce, which would be an exception of the whole from the whole (استثناء الكل من الكل), which is invalid. Al-Minaḥ clarifies this: the restriction to ratls is due to this risk; if someone excepts a single ratl, it is permissible by agreement. The underlying reasoning (talil) is that the exception of a small quantity from a large amount is safe from gharar, but larger unspecified exceptions risk uncertainty about whether anything remains for the buyer.

Key Terms
استثناء قدر معلوم (istithnā' qadr maʿlūm): Excluding a known quantity (e.g., weight) from the object of sale; a legal exception that must be known to avoid gharar.
رطل (raṭl): A unit of weight; the standard measure for fruit exceptions in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is excepting a single ratl from a fruit sale valid by consensus?
A) Because a single ratl is a standard measure.
B) Because it is an exception of a little from a lot, avoiding the risk of excepting the whole.
C) Because the seller has the right to except any amount.
D) Because a single ratl is too small to be considered.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a known small exception from a sale is valid, but the exception must be precisely defined and must not risk being the entire object of sale.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Selling Fruit with Exception of Known Quantity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on exception from fruit sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on selling fruit with an exception of a known quantity and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi position permits excepting known fruit from trees by analogy with grain piles – later jurists overrode the weak transmission.

This passage dives into an internal Hanafi debate about whether a farmer can sell the fruit on his trees but keep a specified portion for himself. The base text says no, but the commentary reveals a more nuanced and authoritative yes. Understanding this dispute teaches you how later Hanafi jurists resolve contradictions between transmissions and why the operative fatwa may differ from a textbook ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the matn's ruling
The base text (matn) states that selling fruit on trees with an exception of a known quantity is invalid. Note the reason given: 'perhaps nothing remains after the excepted quantity.' This is your starting point.
The matn reflects one transmission, not necessarily the authoritative one.
2
Recognize the weak transmission
The commentary attributes the matn's ruling to Riwayat al-Hasan (a non-standard transmission) and al-Tahawi. This is not part of Zahir al-Riwayah, the core authoritative collection.
3
Compare the positions of al-Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaykhayn) hold the sale corrupt whether the tree is bounded or unbounded, because future growth is unknown and guaranteed by the seller. Imam Muhammad permits it for bounded trees by istihsan, considering it a customary condition.
Bounded tree = the fruit is already fully visible and measurable.
4
Understand the Zahir al-Riwayah position
On Zahir al-Riwayah, the sale is permissible even without boundedness. The proof is qiyas (analogy) with selling a specific quantity from a large undifferentiated pile (e.g., a qafiz from a stack of grain). The exception is a known quantity, so no gharar.
This view was chosen by al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, and al-Multaqa.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical effect
Later Hanafi authorities settled on permissibility, following either Muhammad's view (for bounded trees) or the stronger Zahir al-Riwayah (generally). The sale is valid, and the seller keeps the excepted fruit. Fatwa is given accordingly.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the invalid view the contract is rescinded; under the valid view it is enforced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كاستثناء قدر معلوم منها. يقال : صفقة في صفقة. وأجيب بأنه صفقة فاسدة في صفقة صحيحة، ففسدتا جميعاً، وفيه: إنّ الصغرى ممنوعة لما صرَّحوا من أن إجارة النخيل باطلة، والباطل معدوم، ولا يصلح متضمناً، فَتَدَبَّر. مطلقاً . و حكم فساد البيع عند الشيخين سواء تناهى عظمها أو لا ؛ لأنّ ما زادَ وحدث من التّركِ في ملك البائع مضمون عند البيع، وهو مجهول، وقال محمد : لا يفسد في المتناهية مستحسناً ؛ لأنه شرط متعارف ، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة. وفي «البحر» نقلاً عن «الأسرار»: الفتوى على قول محمد، وبـه أخذ الطحاوي ، وفي «المنتقى»: ضم إليه أبو يوسف، وفي «التحفة» : والصحيحُ قولهما ؛ لأن التعامل لم يكن بشرط الترك، وإنما كان بالإذن بالترك من غير شرط. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». قوله : قدر معلوم ؛ قال صاحب «الكنز»، و«التنوير»، و«القُدُوري» : أرطالاً معلومة، وذكر بعض شرّاح «الهداية»: إن المستثنى لو كان رطلاً واحداً يجوز ؛ لأنه استثنى القليل من الكثير بخلاف الأرطال بجواز أن لا يكون إلا ذلك القدر، فيكون استثناء الكلِّ من الكل. وقال في «المنح»: «وقيد بالأرطال ؛ لأنه لو استثنى رطلاً واحداً جاز اتفاقاً». انتهى. ثم: أي باعَ الثَّمَرَ على النَّخْلِ واستثنى قدراً معلوماً لا يجوز البيع ؛ لأنه ربما لا يبقى شيء بعد المستثنى. قوله : لا يجوز البيع ؛ قال في «الهداية»: «قالوا : هذا رواية الحسن ، وهو قول الطحاوي، وأما على ظاهر الروايةِ ينبغي أن يجوز ؛ لأن الأصل إنما يجوز إيرادِ العقد عليه بانفراده، ويجوز استثناؤه من العقد، وبيع قفيـز مـن صـبرة جائز ، فكذا استثناؤه، وبخلاف استثناء الحمل وأطراف الحيوان ؛ لأنه لا يجوز بيعه، فكذا استثناؤه». انتهى. وفي «الكنز»، و«التنوير»، و«الملتقى» : اختاروا ظاهر الرواية.
Like excepting a known quantity from it. It is said: a transaction within a transaction. And it was answered that it is a corrupt transaction within a valid transaction, so both become corrupt. And it contains: the minor premise is prevented because they explicitly stated that leasing palm trees is invalid, and the invalid is non-existent, and it is not suitable to be a constituent, so reflect. Absolutely. And the ruling of the corruption of the sale according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is the same whether its magnitude reaches a limit or not, because what increases and occurs from the leaving in the seller's ownership is guaranteed at the sale, and it is unknown. And Muhammad said: it does not become corrupt in the finite (case) by way of istihsan, because it is a customary condition, and it is the saying of the three imams. And in 'al-Bahr' quoting from 'al-Asrar': the fatwa is on the saying of Muhammad, and al-Tahawi took it. And in 'al-Muntaqa': Abu Yusuf joined him. And in 'al-Tuhfa': the correct is their saying (of the two, i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the practice was not on the condition of leaving, but rather on permission to leave without a condition. Likewise in 'Majma' al-Anhur'. His saying: a known quantity; the author of 'al-Kanz', 'al-Tanwir', and 'al-Quduri' said: known ratls. And some commentators of 'al-Hidayah' mentioned: if the excepted (quantity) were one ratl, it is permissible, because one excepts the little from the much, unlike ratls, because it is possible that it is only that quantity, thus it becomes excepting the whole from the whole. And it is said in 'al-Minh': 'It is restricted to ratls, because if one excepts one ratl, it is permissible by agreement.' End. Then: That is, he sold the fruit on the palm trees and excepted a known quantity – the sale is not permissible, because perhaps nothing remains after the excepted (quantity). His saying: the sale is not permissible; he said in 'al-Hidayah': 'They said: this is the narration of al-Hasan, and it is the saying of al-Tahawi. But according to the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah), it should be permissible, because the principle is that entering the contract upon it alone is permissible, and excepting it from the contract is permissible, and selling a qafiz from a pile is permissible, so likewise excepting it. And unlike excepting the fetus and limbs of animals, because selling them is not permissible, so likewise excepting them.' End. And in 'al-Kanz', 'al-Tanwir', and 'al-Multaqa': they chose the apparent transmission.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of selling fruit on palm trees while excepting a known quantity (e.g., a certain weight). The base text (matn) states the sale is invalid because there is a risk that nothing remains after the exception. However, the commentary expands, showing a significant division within the school. The ruling that the sale is corrupt is attributed to a non-standard transmission (Riwayat al-Hasan). On the authority of Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad), the sale is actually permissible. The reasoning is analogical: just as it is valid to sell a specific quantity from a larger undifferentiated pile (e.g., a qafiz from a stack), it should be valid here. The condition of leaving the fruit is a customary practice, not an invalid stipulation. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) held the sale corrupt if the tree is unbounded, but Imam Muhammad made an exception for bounded trees via istihsan. Over time, later authorities like al-Tahawi and the authors of al-Bahr, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa favored the permissibility view, and it became the mufta bihi (operative position). This illustrates how later Hanafi jurists resolved internal ikhtilaf by preferring the stronger transmission and practical custom.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): The legal act of excluding a specific, known part from a sale contract, such as excluding a certain weight of fruit from the trees being sold.
قدر معلوم (qadr ma'lūm): A known, specified quantity (e.g., a certain number of ratls) that is excepted from the sale.
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The established, authoritative transmission of the Hanafi school as recorded in the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwayah), what is the ruling on selling fruit on palm trees while excepting a known quantity?
A) The sale is invalid because of gharar.
B) The sale is permissible by analogy with selling a qafiz from a stack.
C) The sale is valid only if the tree is bounded.
D) The sale is invalid because it is akin to selling unborn fruit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi permits selling fruit on trees with an exception of a known quantity, based on Zahir al-Riwayah and the choice of later scholars, overriding the weak transmission of Riwayat al-Hasan.

Costs of Measurement and Payment in Sales
Assigning costs of delivery-related actions to seller and buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for assigning costs of measurement, weighing, counting, surveying, and payment in a sales contract.

Core Idea
The seller pays to deliver the goods; the buyer pays to deliver the price.

In a sales transaction, various incidental costs arise when measuring, weighing, or counting the item sold and when paying the price. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on who bears each cost. The principle is simple: each party is responsible for the costs necessary to fulfill their own obligation—delivery of the sold item for the seller, and payment of the price for the buyer. Understanding this allocation is essential for both contracting parties and for muftis advising on sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core obligations
Recognize that the seller must deliver the sold item (al-mabī‘) and the buyer must deliver the price (al-thaman). These are separate wajib acts.
2
List the actions needed for each delivery
For the sold item: measuring volume (kayl), weighing (wazn), counting (‘add), and surveying (dhar‘). For the price: weighing (wazn) and tendering (naqd, which includes checking for counterfeit).
Naqd here means the act of paying sound coins and rejecting defective ones.
3
Apply the maxim: 'What an obligation cannot be fulfilled without is itself obligatory'
Since delivery cannot be completed without these preparatory acts, they become wajib on the party responsible for delivery. Therefore, the seller bears the costs for the sold item, and the buyer bears the costs for the price.
This is a classic Hanafi talil: mā lā yatimm al-wājib illā bihī fa-huwa wājib.
4
Distinguish between weighing the sold item and weighing the price
The text explicitly separates the two: the seller pays for weighing oil (sold item), the buyer pays for weighing the dirhams (price). Even if both involve 'weighing,' the object determines the responsible party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجرة الكيل والوزن والدرع والعددِ على البائع ، وأجرةُ وزنِ الثَّمَنِ ونقده على المشتري. قوله : وأجرة الكيل ؛ في مثل البرِّ للكيال، وأُجرة الوزن في مثل الزيت للوزان، وأجرة العدّ في مثل الغنم للعدّاد، وأُجرةُ الدرع في مثل الأرض للذراع على البائع فيما بيع بشرط الكيلِ والوزن والعد والذرع ؛ لأنّ التسليم واجب على البائع، ولا يحصل إلا بالكيل ومثله، وما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب. قوله : ونقده ؛ أي نقد الثمن، والنقد : بالفتح آماده كرون و دادن وهره كرون درهم ودينار. كذا في «المنتخب»، والثالث هو المراد، ويقال : نقدتُ الدراهم وانتقدتها : أي أخرجت منها الزيف. كذا في «الصراح» فمعناه باللغة الهندية : يركهنا. قوله : على المشتري ؛ لأنّ الوزن من تمام التسليم، فيجب عليه، وكذا يجب
And the fee for measuring, weighing, tending, and counting is on the seller, and the fee for weighing the price and its tender is on the buyer. His saying: And the fee for measuring; for example, in wheat, for the measurer, and the fee for weighing in oil for the weigher, and the fee for counting in sheep for the counter, and the fee for tending in land for the surveyor is on the seller, in what is sold with the condition of measuring, weighing, counting, and surveying; because delivery is obligatory on the seller, and it is only accomplished by measuring and the like, and what an obligation cannot be fulfilled except by it is itself obligatory. His saying: and its tender; that is, the tender of the price, and al-naqd: with fath (of the first letter) is 'amadeh kron' and 'dadad' and 'hreh kron' dirhams and dinars. Thus in 'al-Muntakhab', and the third is intended, and it is said: naqadtu al-darāhima wa intaqadtuhā, i.e., I removed the counterfeit ones. Thus in 'al-Sirāh' so its meaning in the Hindi language is 'yarkhuna'. His saying: on the buyer; because weighing is part of perfecting delivery, so it is obligatory on him, and likewise is obligatory...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage provides a clear ruling on who bears the costs associated with fulfilling a sale contract. The base text states that the seller pays for measuring, weighing, counting, and surveying (tending the land) of the sold item, while the buyer pays for weighing and tendering the price. The commentary elaborates: the seller must deliver the sold item (al-mabī‘), and since delivery (taslīm) cannot be completed without actions like measuring grain, weighing oil, counting sheep, or surveying land, these costs fall on the seller. This is derived from the principle 'what an obligation cannot be fulfilled without is itself obligatory' (mā lā yatimm al-wājib illā bihī fa-huwa wājib). On the other hand, the buyer must deliver the price (thaman), so weighing the price and checking for counterfeit coins (naqd) are the buyer's responsibility. The text also explains the term 'naqd' as meaning the act of tendering sound dirhams and dinars, or removing counterfeit ones. This ruling is straightforward and consistent across the Hanafi school, with no significant internal disagreement noted.

Key Terms
الكيل (al-kayl): The act of measuring volume (e.g., for grains); the fee for this service is on the seller.
الوزن (al-wazn): The act of weighing, whether of the sold item (on seller) or of the price (on buyer).
النقد (al-naqd): The tender or payment of the price, specifically the act of paying sound coins and rejecting counterfeit ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who bears the fee for weighing the sold item (e.g., oil) according to the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer, because weighing is part of payment.
B) The seller, because delivery is his obligation and weighing is necessary for delivery.
C) The seller, only if stipulated in the contract.
D) The buyer, because he benefits from accuracy.

Key Takeaway
In a sale, the seller bears costs for delivering the sold item (measuring, weighing, etc.), and the buyer bears costs for delivering the price (weighing and tendering).

Allocation of Measurement and Payment Costs in Sales
Ruling on allocation of measurement and payment costs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seller's and buyer's respective obligations for measurement and payment costs in a sale and apply the principle that what is necessary for an obligation becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Seller pays for delivering goods; buyer pays for verifying price.

In any sale, certain costs are necessary to complete the transaction. The Hanafi school assigns these costs based on who is responsible for each part of the delivery and payment. This ruling clarifies that the seller bears the expenses of measuring, weighing, surveying, and counting the sold items, while the buyer bears the cost of weighing and examining the price. The underlying principle is that whatever is essential to fulfill an obligatory duty becomes obligatory itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their obligations
Read the passage and note that the seller is responsible for costs related to the goods (measuring, weighing, etc.) and the buyer for costs related to the price (weighing and examining).
2
Define the four types of measurement
Understand each term: kayl (volume for grains), wazn (weight for liquids), dhira' (cubit for land), add (counting for livestock). The seller pays for these when the sale is conditioned on such measurement.
3
Understand the buyer's cost: naqd
Naqd means both paying and checking the coins for authenticity. The buyer must ensure the price is valid, so the cost of this verification is on the buyer.
4
Apply the legal principle
The commentary states: 'because delivery is obligatory upon the seller, and it is not achieved except by measuring... and whatever is necessary to fulfill an obligation becomes obligatory.' This is a key Hanafi legal maxim (al-wajib la yatimmu illa bihi fa-huwa wajib). Apply it to understand the reasoning.
5
Contrast with seller's duties
Note that if the sold item itself requires weighing or measuring for delivery (e.g., selling by weight), the seller also bears those costs because they are part of completing delivery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجرة الكيل والوزن والدرع والعدد على البائع، وأجرة وزن الثمن ونقده على المشتري. [٢] قوله : وأجرة الكيل؛ في مثل البرِّ للكيال، وأجرة الوزن في مثل الزيت للوزان، وأجرة العدّ في مثل الغنم للعدّاد، وأجرةُ الدرع في مثل الأرض للذراع على البائع فيما بيع بشرط الكيل والوزن والعد والذرع؛ لأنّ التسليم واجب على البائع، ولا يحصل إلا بالكيل ومثله، وما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب. [٣] قوله : ونقده؛ أي نقد الثمن، والنقد : بالفتح آماده كرون و دادن وهره كرون درهم ودينار. كذا في «المنتخب»، والثالث هو المراد، ويقال : نقدتُ الدراهم وانتقدتها : أي أخرجت منها الزيف. كذا في «الصراح» فمعناه باللغة الهندية : يركهنا. [٤] قوله : على المشتري؛ لأنّ الوزن من تمام التسليم، فيجب عليه، وكذا يجب فيما بيع مكايلة وكذا أجرة وزن المبيع وذرعه وعده على البائع؛ لأن هذه الأشياء من تمام التسليم، وهو على البائع، فكذا تمامه. ينظر : رمز الحقائق (٢ : ٨).
And the fee for measuring, weighing, measuring by cubit, and counting is upon the seller, and the fee for weighing the price and its payment is upon the buyer. [2] His saying: 'And the fee for measuring' – for example, for wheat, the measurer; the fee for weighing, like for oil, the weigher; the fee for counting, like for sheep, the counter; the fee for measuring by cubit, like for land, the surveyor – is upon the seller in what is sold with the condition of measuring, weighing, counting, and surveying, because delivery is obligatory upon the seller, and it is not achieved except by measuring and the like, and whatever is necessary to fulfill an obligation becomes obligatory. [3] His saying: 'and its payment' – meaning the payment of the price; 'naqd' with fath (a) means coins such as dirhams and dinars. As in al-Muntakhab, and the third meaning is intended; it is said: 'I paid the dirhams and examined them' meaning I removed the counterfeit from them. As in al-Sirah, so its meaning in the Hindi language is 'to weigh'. [4] His saying: 'upon the buyer' – because weighing is part of completing delivery, so it is obligatory upon him, and similarly it is obligatory in what is sold by measuring, and likewise the fee for weighing the sold item, measuring it by cubit, and counting it is upon the seller, because these things are part of completing delivery, and that is upon the seller, so its completion is likewise. Reference: Ramz al-Haqa'iq (2:8).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the distribution of costs associated with the execution of a sale contract in Hanafi fiqh. The text is taken from a commentary on the matn, explaining the base ruling. The ruling distinguishes between costs for delivering the sold goods (seller's responsibility) and costs for verifying the price (buyer's responsibility). The seller bears the expenses of measuring (kayl), weighing (wazn), surveying (dhira'), and counting (add) because these are necessary for delivering the goods as required. The buyer bears the expense of weighing the price (zann al-thaman) and examining it (naqd) because these are part of the buyer's duty to ensure the price is correct. The commentary clarifies the types of goods corresponding to each measurement and the underlying legal principle: whatever is essential for fulfilling an obligation becomes obligatory itself.

Key Terms
الكيل (al-kayl): Measurement by volume, e.g., for grains like wheat.
الوزن (al-wazn): Measurement by weight, e.g., for liquids like oil.
الدرع (al-dhira'): Measurement by cubit, e.g., for land.
العدد (al-add): Counting by number, e.g., for livestock like sheep.
النقد (al-naqd): The act of paying and examining coins to ensure they are genuine.
التسليم (al-taslim): Delivery of the sold item to the buyer in a manner that enables possession.

Selling Fruit with an Exception: Valid or Invalid?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the preferred and non-preferred Hanafi views on selling fruit with an exception, and apply the qiyas reasoning used in the preferred view.

Core Idea
Selling fruit with an exception is valid in the preferred Hanafi view, analogously to selling a qafiz from a stack.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about selling fruit on trees while excluding a known quantity for the seller. The initial ruling seems to prohibit it due to uncertainty, but a deeper look reveals a stronger opinion that validates it by analogy. We will explore the reasoning and weigh the positions.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the subject matter: selling fruit on trees and excluding a known quantity (e.g., one basket for the seller). The core question is whether such an exception renders the sale invalid.
2
Identify the Two Positions
There is a weaker narration (riwayat al-Hasan) and al-Tahawi who say the sale is invalid because nothing may remain after the exception. The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and the majority of later texts say it is valid.
3
Follow the Qiyas Argument
The preferred view uses qiyas: selling a qafiz from a pile (subrah) is valid, so excluding a known amount from fruit on trees should also be valid. The key distinction is that the exception itself must be a permissible subject of sale (like the qafiz), unlike a fetus or animal limb which are not independently saleable.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas, as the illah is the ability to single out the excluded item.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The later authoritative texts—al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, al-Multaqa—explicitly choose the view of zahir al-riwaya. Therefore, the fatwa today is that the sale is valid (sahih).
The opposing view is not completely discarded but is considered weaker (marjuh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَي باعَ الثَّمَرَ على النَّخْلِ واستثنى قدراً معلوماً لا يجوز البيع؛ لأنه ربما لا يبقى شيء بعد المستثنى. [1] قوله : لا يجوز البيع؛ قال في «الهداية»: «قالوا: هذا رواية الحسن، وهو قول الطحاوي له، وأما على ظاهر الروايةِ ينبغي أن يجوز؛ لأن الأصل إنما يجوز إيرادِ العقد عليه بانفراده، ويجوز استثناؤه من العقد، وبيع قفيـز مـن صـبرة جائز، فكذا استثناؤه، وبخلاف استثنائه الحمل وأطراف الحيوان؛ لأنه لا يجوز بيعه، فكذا استثناؤه». انتهى. وفي «الكنز»، و«التنوير»، و«الملتقى»: اختاروا ظاهر الرواية.
That is, if one sells fruit on the palm trees and excludes a known quantity, the sale is not permissible because perhaps nothing remains after the exclusion. [1] His saying: 'The sale is not permissible'; he said in al-Hidayah: 'They said: This is the narration of al-Hasan, and it is the saying of al-Tahawi. But according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), it should be permissible because the original principle is that the contract is concluded on it alone, and excluding it from the contract is permissible. And selling a qafiz from a stack is permissible, so likewise its exclusion. And it differs from excluding the fetus and the limbs of an animal because its sale is not permissible, so likewise its exclusion.' End. And in al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, and al-Multaqa: they chose the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of selling fruit on trees while excluding a known quantity for the seller. The text first states a general ruling that such a sale is invalid because there is a risk that nothing remains after the exclusion. The commentary then cites al-Hidayah, which explains that this is a weak narration (riwayat al-Hasan) and the opinion of al-Tahawi, but the preferred view (zahir al-riwaya) is that the sale is valid. The reasoning is analogical (qiyas): just as selling a specific qafiz (measure) from a larger pile of grain is valid, excluding a known amount from a sale of fruit on trees should also be valid. The difference is that excluding a fetus or animal limbs is invalid because those cannot be sold independently. The later authoritative texts (al-Kanz, al-Tanwir, al-Multaqa) adopt the preferred view.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Excluding a known quantity from the subject matter of a sale.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from Abu Hanifa and his companions in the six authoritative books.
رواية الحسن (riwayat al-Hasan): Narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, which may be weaker than the zahir al-riwayah.
قفيز (qafiz): A specific volume measure, used in analogy for selling a quantity from a larger pile.
صبرة (subrah): A pile or heap of goods (e.g., grain) from which a part can be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is invalid because nothing may remain
B) The sale is valid like selling a qafiz from a pile
C) The sale is valid only if the trees are specified
D) The sale is invalid because the fruit is not yet ripe

Key Takeaway
Selling fruit on trees with a known exception is valid in the preferred Hanafi view, based on clear qiyas with selling a qafiz from a pile.

Delivery Order and Payment Expense: Hanafi Rulings and Intra-School Disputes
Ruling on sequence of delivery and intra-Hanafi dispute on payment expense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the sequence of delivery in a price sale and the expense of payment (naqd), and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute and its mufta bihi.

Core Idea
In a price sale, the buyer pays first; the cost of paying is on the seller per the preferred view.

When a commodity is sold for cash, who delivers first, and who bears the cost of payment? The Hanafi school has clear rules: the price is delivered first because the goods become specified by the sale. However, within the school, there is disagreement on who pays the expense of examining and delivering the price. The later scholars settled on the seller. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof, the dispute with Shafi'i, and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What is the ruling on the sequence of delivery in a price sale? The text states: 'the price is delivered first' when the sold item is present. Determine the hukm level (obligatory, recommended, etc.).
The ruling is a contractual obligation; violating it by condition may invalidate the sale.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
What proof is given for paying first? The text says: 'because the commodity becomes specified by the sale.' Analyse how specification (ta'yin) justifies the order.
The proof is a legal reasoning (ta'lil) based on the effect of the contract.
3
Note the Cross-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing view: al-Shafi'i in one opinion holds simultaneous delivery. How does the Hanafi school respond? The Hanafi response is that paying first achieves equality in specifying each party's right.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in a Hanafi context, the buyer pays first even if the goods are present.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Expense
List the three narrations regarding who bears the cost of naqd: (1) Ibn Sami'a: on seller, (2) Ibn Rustom: on seller, (3) Muhammad: on debt owner after possession. Compare them.
The first two agree; the third adds a condition of possession.
5
Determine the Tarjih (Relied Position)
According to al-Fatawa al-Sughra and al-Waqi'at, the mashayikh gave fatwa that the cost of naqd is on the seller. Identify the authority weight (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
This is the mufta bihi position for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي بيع سلعة بثمن سلم هو أولاً، وفي غيره سلماً معاً) : أي في بيع السلعة بالثَّمَن : أي بالدراهم والدنانير سلَّمَ الثَّمَنَ أَوَّلاً، لأَنَّ السِّلْعَةَ تتعين بالبيع، عليه تسليم الجيد، فيكون أجرةً من يميّز ذلك عليه، وهذه رواية ابن سماعة له عن محمد الله، وفي رواية ابن رستم الله عنه أجرة نقدِ الثّمن على البائع؛ لأنّ النقد يكون بعد التسليم والوزن. وعن محمد الله : إِنَّ أجرة النقدِ على ربِّ الدين بعد القبض، وقبله على المديون، وفي «الفتاوى الصغرى»: اختلف المشايخ في أجرةِ النَّقد، قال بعضهم: على البائع، وقال بعضهم : على المشتري، ثم قال : وبه أفتى واختاره في «الواقعات»، كذا ذكره العلامة العَيْنِي في «شرح الكنز»). [1] قوله : سلم؛ في «الدر المختار»: «التسليم يكون بالتخلية على وجه يتمكن من القبض بلا مانع ولا حائل، وشرط في الأجناس شرطاً ثالثاً وهو أن يقول : خليت بينك وبين المبيع ، فلو لم يقله أو كان بعيداً لم يصر قابضاً، والناس عنه غافلون، فإنهم يشترون قرية ويقرون بالتسليم والقبض، وهو لا يصح به القبض على الصحيح، وكذا الهبة». انتهى. [٢) قوله : سلّم الثمن أوَّلاً؛ أي قبل المبيع، يعني إذا وقع المنازعة بينهما في تسليم المبيع والثَّمن قيل للمشتري : ادفع الثَّمن أوّلاً؛ لأنَّ حق المشتري تعين في المبيع، فيقدم دفع الثَّمن ليتعين في البائع في القبض لما أنه يتعين بالتعيين تحقيقاً للمساواة في تعيين حق كل واحدٍ منهما، خلافاً للشافعي له في قول، هذا إذا كان المبيع حاضراً. وإن غائباً فلا يُسَلِّمُ حتى يُحْضِرَ البائع المبيع على مثال الراهن مع المرتهن. كذا في الأنهر». وفي «الفتاوى البَزَّازية»: «باع بشرط أن يدفع المبيع قبل نقد الثَّمن فسد البيع؛ لأنه لا يقتضيه العقد، وقال محمد الله : لا يصح لجهالة الأجل، حتى لو سمى الوقت الذي يُسَلِّمُ فيه المبيع «جاز». انتهى.
And in the sale of a commodity for a price, delivery is first; in other cases, delivery is simultaneous. That is, in the sale of the commodity for the price, meaning for dirhams and dinars, the price is delivered first because the commodity becomes specified by the sale; upon him is to deliver the good (currency), so the fee for one who distinguishes that is upon him. And this is the narration of Ibn Sami'a from Muhammad (al-Shaybani). And in the narration of Ibn Rustom from him, the fee for payment of the price is upon the seller because payment occurs after delivery and weighing. And from Muhammad: Indeed, the fee for payment is upon the owner of the debt after taking possession, and before that upon the debtor. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The mashayikh differed regarding the fee for payment; some said it is upon the seller, some said upon the buyer. Then he said: 'And this is what he gave fatwa and chose in al-Waqi'at.' As mentioned by Allama al-Ayni in Sharh al-Kanz. [1] His saying: 'delivery' – in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'Delivery is by vacating in a manner that enables taking possession without obstacle or barrier, and for goods a third condition is that he says: I have vacated between you and the sold item; if he does not say it or if he remains distant, he does not become a possessor, and people are heedless of this, for they buy a village and affirm delivery and possession, but possession is not valid by that according to the correct view, and similarly for gifts.' End. [2] His saying: ‘He delivered the price first’ – meaning before the goods; that is, if a dispute occurs between them regarding delivery of the goods and the price, the buyer is told to pay the price first because the buyer’s right has been specified in the goods, so he advances paying the price to make it specified for the seller in receipt, because it becomes specified by specification to achieve equality in specifying each party’s right, contrary to al-Shafi’i in one opinion. This is if the goods are present. If absent, he does not deliver until the seller brings the goods similar to a pawner with a pawnbroker. As in al-Anhur. In al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya: ‘If he sold on condition that he deliver the goods before payment of the price, the sale is invalid because the contract does not require that; Muhammad said: It is not valid due to ignorance of the term, unless he specifies the time for delivering the goods, then it is valid.’ End.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: the sequence of delivery in a sale and the expense of paying the price. The first part states a general rule: when a commodity is sold for a price (dirhams/dinars), the price is delivered first because the commodity becomes specified by the sale. However, in other types of sales, delivery is simultaneous. The commentary then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding who bears the cost of paying the price (naqd). Three narrations are given: (1) Ibn Sami'a says the cost is on the seller, (2) Ibn Rustom says it is on the seller because payment comes after delivery, (3) Muhammad al-Shaybani says it is on the owner of the debt after possession. The later fatwa, according to al-Fatawa al-Sughra, chose the view that the cost is on the seller. The text then defines delivery (taslim) from al-Durr al-Mukhtar, emphasizing the need for actual vacating. Finally, it discusses a dispute between buyer and seller: the buyer pays first if the goods are present; if absent, the seller must bring the goods. A condition to deliver goods before payment invalidates the sale unless a time is specified.

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslim): Delivery by vacating the premises to enable the buyer to take possession without obstruction.
النقد (al-naqd): Payment of the price, including examining the coins for quality.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification of the sold item by the contract; it determines the buyer's right.
المبيع حاضر (al-mabi' hadir): The sold item is present at the place of contract, allowing immediate delivery.
المبيع غائب (al-mabi' gha'ib): The sold item is absent; the seller must bring it before the buyer pays.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) It depends on who initiates the payment
D) It is shared equally

Key Takeaway
In a price sale, the buyer pays first, and the cost of paying (naqd) is borne by the seller according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa.

Order of Payment and Expense of Currency Checking in Sales
Commentary on order of delivery and currency checking expense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who must pay first when goods are present and determine the expense of checking currency based on the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
When goods are present, the buyer pays first; the expense of checking currency is on the buyer.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a practical dispute in sales: when goods are present, who must deliver first? The Hanafi school rules that the buyer must pay the price first because his right becomes fixed in the specific commodity. The text also explores the expense of verifying the currency (ujrat al-naqd), where multiple narrations from Imam Muhammad exist, but the later fatwa places it on the buyer. Understanding these rulings helps avoid riba and ensures fairness in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenarios
The passage discusses two main issues: (1) order of delivery when a dispute arises and goods are present, and (2) who bears the expense of checking currency. Separate these in your mind.
Also note the distinction between sales for money, barter, and currency exchange.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling on order of delivery
When the sold commodity is present and a dispute occurs, the buyer must pay first. The reason: the buyer's right is specified (ta'ayyun) in the commodity, so payment is advanced to achieve equality in specification.
This is contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i's view.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on ujrat al-naqd
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani has three narrations: (a) expense on seller (Ibn Sama'ah), (b) expense on seller (Ibn Rustam), (c) after possession on owner of the debt, before on debtor. The later fatwa (al-Fatawa al-Sughra, al-Waqi'at) chooses the view that it is on the buyer.
The mufta bihi is on the buyer, despite some earlier narrations.
4
Define delivery (taslim) according to al-Durr al-Mukhtar
Delivery requires vacating (takhliya) enabling possession without obstacle. For commodities, an additional verbal condition (saying 'khallaytu') is needed. This clarifies what constitutes valid possession.
People often neglect this verbal condition, but it is essential for valid delivery.
5
Apply the rulings to special cases
If goods are absent, the buyer does not pay until the seller brings them. A condition to deliver goods before payment invalidates the sale unless a specific time is set. In barter and currency exchange, both deliver together.
Riba is avoided by ensuring specification of both countervalues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و في بيع سلعة بثمن سلم . هو أولاً ، وفي غيره سلماً معاً) : أي في بيع السلعة بالثَّمَن : أي بالدراهم والدنانير سلَّمَ الثَّمَنَ أَوَّلاً ، لأَنَّ السِّلْعَةَ تتعين بالبيع ، عليه تسليم الجيد، فيكون أجرةً من يميّز ذلك عليه ، وهذه رواية ابن سماعة له عن محمد الله، وفي رواية ابن رستم الله عنه أجرة نقدِ الثّمن على البائع ؛ لأنّ النقد يكون بعد التسليم والوزن. وعن محمد الله : إِنَّ أجرة النقدِ على ربِّ الدين بعد القبض، وقبله على المديون، وفي «الفتاوى الصغرى»: اختلف المشايخ في أجرةِ النَّقد، قال بعضهم: على البائع، وقال بعضهم : على المشتري، ثم قال : وبه أفتى واختاره في «الواقعات»، كذا ذكره العلامة العَيْنِي في «شرح الكنز»). قوله : سلم ; في «الدر المختار»: «التسليم يكون بالتخلية على وجه يتمكن من القبض بلا مانع ولا حائل، وشرط في الأجناس شرطاً ثالثاً وهو أن يقول : خليت بينك وبين المبيع ، فلو لم يقله أو كان بعيداً لم يصر قابضاً، والناس عنه غافلون، فإنهم يشترون قرية ويقرون بالتسليم والقبض، وهو لا يصح به القبض على الصحيح، وكذا الهبة». انتهى . قوله : سلّم الثمن أوَّلاً ؛ أي قبل المبيع، يعني إذا وقع المنازعة بينهما في تسليم المبيع والثَّمن قيل للمشتري : ادفع الثَّمن أوّلاً ؛ لأنَّ حق المشتري تعين في المبيع، فيقدم دفع الثَّمن ليتعين في البائع في القبض لما أنه يتعين بالتعيين تحقيقاً للمساواة في تعيين حق كل واحدٍ منهما، خلافاً للشافعي له في قول، هذا إذا كان المبيع حاضراً. وإن غائباً فلا يُسَلِّمُ حتى يُحْضِرَ البائع المبيع على مثال الراهن مع المرتهن. كذا في الأنهر. وفي «الفتاوى البَزَّازية»: «باع بشرط أن يدفع المبيع قبل نقد الثَّمن فسد البيع ؛ لأنه لا يقتضيه العقد، وقال محمد الله : لا يصح لجهالة الأجل، حتى لو سمى الوقت الذي يُسَلِّمُ فيه المبيع جاز». انتهى . والدراهم والدنانير لا تتعيَّنُ إلا بالتسليم فلا بُدَّ من تَعَيَّنه ؛ لئلا يلزم الربا. أو في غيره : أَي في بيع السلعة بالسِّلْعَةِ، وهو بيع المقايضة، وفي بيع الثَّمَنِ بِالثَّمَن : أَي الصَّرْفُ سُلِّما معا لتساويهما في التَّعَيُّن وعدمه. العين. قوله : لئلا يلزم الربا ; أي ألا يلزم فضل أحد البدلين ; لأنَّ الدين أنقص من العين.
And in selling a commodity for a price (payment of price first) — he delivers the price first, and in other [types] they deliver together. That is: in selling a commodity for a price, i.e. for dirhams and dinars, he pays the price first, because the commodity becomes specified by the sale. He must deliver the good [quality], so the expense of distinguishing that is upon him. This is the narration of Ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad [al-Shaybani]. In the narration of Ibn Rustam from him, the expense of checking the price is upon the seller, because checking occurs after delivery and weighing. And from Muhammad: The expense of checking the price is upon the owner of the debt after possession, and before possession it is upon the debtor. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The scholars differed concerning the expense of checking; some said it is upon the seller, some said upon the buyer, then he said: 'And with this [view] I give fatwa and he chose it in al-Waqi'at.' As mentioned by al-Allamah al-Ayni in Sharh al-Kanz. He said: 'Salam (delivery): in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: Delivery is by vacating in a manner that enables possession without any obstacle or barrier, and a third condition for commodities is that he says: I have vacated between you and the sold item. If he does not say it or is far, he does not become a possessor. People are heedless of this; they buy a village and affirm delivery and possession, but possession is not valid by that according to the correct view, and likewise for gift.' End. He said: 'He delivers the price first, i.e. before the sold item. That is, if a dispute arises between them regarding delivery of the sold item and the price, the buyer is told: Pay the price first because the buyer's right is specified in the sold item, so payment of the price is advanced to specify it for the seller in possession, since it becomes specified by specification, achieving equality in specification of each party's right. Contrary to al-Shafi'i — he has a [different] view. This is if the sold item is present. If it is absent, he does not pay until the seller brings the sold item, like the case of the pledgor with the pledgee. As in al-Anhar. And in al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya: If he sells with the condition that he deliver the sold item before paying the price, the sale is invalid because the contract does not require it. Muhammad [al-Shaybani] said: It is invalid due to unknown term, unless he specifies the time for delivery — then it is valid.' End. And dirhams and dinars are not specified except by delivery, so their specification is necessary to avoid riba. Or in other: i.e. in selling a commodity for a commodity, which is barter, and in selling a price for a price, i.e. currency exchange, they deliver together due to their equal [status] regarding specification and non-specification. The real [value]. He said: 'Lest riba occur; i.e. lest excess of one of the two countervalues occur, because the debt is less than the real [value].
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling regarding the order of delivery in a sale and the expense of checking the currency (ajrat al-naqd). The author begins by explaining that in a sale of a commodity for a price (i.e., money), the buyer must pay the price first. The rationale is that the commodity becomes specified by the sale contract, so the buyer's right becomes fixed in that specific commodity. The seller must hand over the good quality, so the expense of distinguishing good quality is on the seller. However, regarding the expense of checking the currency (naqd), there are multiple narrations from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. In one narration from Ibn Sama'ah, this expense is on the seller; in another from Ibn Rustam, it is on the seller as well because checking occurs after delivery; but a third narration from Muhammad puts it on the owner of the debt after possession. The later fatwa (as per al-Fatawa al-Sughra) chooses the view that it is on the buyer. The author then quotes al-Durr al-Mukhtar's definition of 'salam' (delivery) as vacating the premises to enable possession, adding a third condition for commodities: the seller must verbally state 'I have vacated' for possession to be valid. Next, the text discusses a dispute between buyer and seller over delivery: if the goods are present, the buyer must pay first, because the buyer's right is in the specific commodity. The Hanafi position differs from Imam al-Shafi'i, who has a different view. If the goods are absent, the buyer should not pay until the seller brings the goods. The text also rules that a condition to deliver the goods before payment invalidates the sale, unless a specific time for delivery is set. Finally, the reason dirhams and dinars must be delivered together in a sale is to avoid riba (excess), since they are not specified except by delivery. The passage ends with a note that in barter and currency exchange, both parties deliver together.

Key Terms
تسليم (taslim): Delivery in a sale, defined in al-Durr al-Mukhtar as vacating the property to enable possession without obstacle.
أجرة النقد (ujrat al-naqd): The expense incurred for checking the currency (e.g., weighing, verifying quality).
تعيين (ta'ayyun): Specification of a thing, e.g., the sold commodity becomes specified by the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a dispute arises between buyer and seller over delivery and the goods are present, who must pay first?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Both simultaneously
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales, when goods are present, the buyer must pay first to equalize specification; the expense of checking currency is ultimately on the buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sales by Subject: Barter, Currency Exchange, Salam, and Ordinary Sale
Enumeration and definition of four sale types by subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of sale categorized by the subject of exchange and distinguish them by what is exchanged.

Core Idea
Barter (goods for goods), sarf (money for money), salam (price now for goods later), and ordinary sale (goods for price).

The Hanafi jurists classified sales by considering what is being exchanged. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya lists four types: barter, currency exchange, salam, and the ordinary sale. Understanding these categories clarifies the juridical nature of each contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
From the passage, list the four types of sale by subject: muqayadah, sarf, salam, and the unnamed ordinary sale.
2
Determine what is exchanged
For each type, identify the exchanged items: muqayadah = commodity for commodity; sarf = money for money; salam = price (paid now) for commodity (delivered later); ordinary = commodity for price (instant).
Salam is an exception because the commodity is not present at contract.
3
Recognize the unique status of salam
Salam is a forward sale where the price is paid immediately but delivery is postponed. This is an exception to the general rule that the object of sale must be existent at the time of contract.
Hanafi school permits salam with strict conditions.
4
Note the unnamed ordinary sale
The fourth type has no special name; it is the default, well-known sale (bay' mutlaq).
This is the most common type of transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بيع السلعة بالسلعة ; اعلم أنَّ البيع باعتبار تعلقه بالمبيع أربعة أنواع ; لأنه إما أن يقع على عين بعين، أو ثمن بثمن، أو ثمن بعين، أو عين بثمن : فالأول : مقايضة. والثاني: صرف. والثالث : سلم. والرابع : ليس له اسم خاص، فهو بيع مطلق مشهور. وباعتبار تعلقه بالثّمن وبمقداره على أربعة أنواع أيضاً ; لأنَّ الثَّمَنَ الأَوَّل إما أن يعتبر أولاً ، وعلى الأوَّل فمع زيادة أو بدونها ، أو مع نقص : فالأول : مرابحة. والثاني : تولية. والثالث : وضيعة. والرابع : مساومة.
He said: 'Selling a commodity for a commodity'; know that sale, according to its connection to the sold item, is of four types: because it either occurs on a specific item for a specific item, or a price for a price, or a price for a specific item, or a specific item for a price. The first: barter (muqayadah). The second: currency exchange (sarf). The third: salam (advance payment). The fourth: has no specific name, so it is the absolute well-known sale. And according to its connection to the price and its amount, it also has four types: because the first price is either considered first, and upon the first, either with increase, or without it, or with decrease. The first: cost-plus (murabahah). The second: partnership (tawliyah). The third: discount (wadi'ah). The fourth: negotiation (musawamah).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of sales. It presents two distinct categorization schemes. First, sales are divided by what is exchanged: (1) barter (muqayada) — exchange of a commodity for a commodity, (2) currency exchange (sarf) — exchange of money for money, (3) salam — advance payment sale where the price is paid now for a commodity to be delivered later, and (4) ordinary sale — a specific item for a price (e.g., buying a shirt for cash). Second, sales are divided by how the price is disclosed: (1) murabahah — sale at cost plus a stated profit, (2) tawliyah — sale at exactly the original cost, (3) wadi'ah — sale at a discount from the original cost, and (4) musawamah — sale by bargaining without reference to the original cost. This classification is foundational for understanding Hanafi sale contracts.

Key Terms
مقايضة (muqayadah): Barter: exchange of a commodity for another commodity, no money involved.
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange: sale of one type of money for another (e.g., gold for silver).
سلم (salam): Advance payment sale: buyer pays the price in full at the time of contract for goods to be delivered at a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of sale involves the exchange of one type of money for another type of money, such as gold coins for silver coins?
A) Muqayadah
B) Sarf
C) Salam
D) Ordinary sale

Key Takeaway
Sales are classified by what is exchanged: commodities for commodities (muqayadah), money for money (sarf), price now for goods later (salam), or goods for price now (ordinary sale).

Sales by Price Disclosure: Murabahah, Tawliyah, Wadi'ah, and Musawamah
Enumeration and definition of four sale types by price mechanism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of sale based on price disclosure and apply the correct type given a commercial scenario.

Core Idea
Sales are classified by how the original cost is disclosed: cost-plus, at cost, at a loss, or without reference to cost.

The second classification in the passage concerns how the price is determined relative to the seller's original cost. This is crucial for trust-based transactions (amanah) and modern Islamic finance. The four types are murabahah (cost + profit), tawliyah (cost price), wadi'ah (cost - loss), and musawamah (bargaining without cost disclosure).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types by price disclosure
From the passage, list: murabahah, tawliyah, wadi'ah, musawamah.
2
Understand the role of the original cost
In murabahah, tawliyah, and wadi'ah, the seller's original cost (thaman awwal) is considered and disclosed. In musawamah, it is not.
3
Distinguish based on price adjustment
Murabahah: original cost plus profit; Tawliyah: exact cost; Wadi'ah: cost minus a discount; Musawamah: no reference to cost.
Murabahah and wadi'ah involve addition or subtraction; tawliyah is neutral.
4
Recognize the trust (amanah) element
Sales that disclose cost (murabahah, tawliyah, wadi'ah) are trust-based; the buyer relies on the seller's honesty. Musawamah is a regular sale.
Hanafi school requires full honesty in cost disclosure for these types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بيع السلعة بالسلعة ; اعلم أنَّ البيع باعتبار تعلقه بالمبيع أربعة أنواع ; لأنه إما أن يقع على عين بعين، أو ثمن بثمن، أو ثمن بعين، أو عين بثمن : فالأول : مقايضة. والثاني: صرف. والثالث : سلم. والرابع : ليس له اسم خاص، فهو بيع مطلق مشهور. وباعتبار تعلقه بالثّمن وبمقداره على أربعة أنواع أيضاً ; لأنَّ الثَّمَنَ الأَوَّل إما أن يعتبر أولاً ، وعلى الأوَّل فمع زيادة أو بدونها ، أو مع نقص : فالأول : مرابحة. والثاني : تولية. والثالث : وضيعة. والرابع : مساومة.
He said: 'Selling a commodity for a commodity'; know that sale, according to its connection to the sold item, is of four types: because it either occurs on a specific item for a specific item, or a price for a price, or a price for a specific item, or a specific item for a price. The first: barter (muqayadah). The second: currency exchange (sarf). The third: salam (advance payment). The fourth: has no specific name, so it is the absolute well-known sale. And according to its connection to the price and its amount, it also has four types: because the first price is either considered first, and upon the first, either with increase, or without it, or with decrease. The first: cost-plus (murabahah). The second: partnership (tawliyah). The third: discount (wadi'ah). The fourth: negotiation (musawamah).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of sales. It presents two distinct categorization schemes. First, sales are divided by what is exchanged: (1) barter (muqayada) — exchange of a commodity for a commodity, (2) currency exchange (sarf) — exchange of money for money, (3) salam — advance payment sale where the price is paid now for a commodity to be delivered later, and (4) ordinary sale — a specific item for a price (e.g., buying a shirt for cash). Second, sales are divided by how the price is disclosed: (1) murabahah — sale at cost plus a stated profit, (2) tawliyah — sale at exactly the original cost, (3) wadi'ah — sale at a discount from the original cost, and (4) musawamah — sale by bargaining without reference to the original cost. This classification is foundational for understanding Hanafi sale contracts.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): Cost-plus sale: seller discloses the original cost and adds a profit margin.
تولية (tawliyah): Sale at original cost: seller transfers the item at the price he paid, without profit or loss.
وضيعة (wadi'ah): Sale at a loss: seller sells below the original cost.
مساومة (musawamah): Bargaining sale: buyer and seller negotiate the price without reference to the original cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of sale is defined as selling at exactly the original cost, with no profit or loss?
A) Murabahah
B) Tawliyah
C) Wadi'ah
D) Musawamah

Key Takeaway
Price-based classification hinges on whether the original cost is disclosed and adjusted: murabahah (profit), tawliyah (cost), wadi'ah (loss), musawamah (no cost reference).

Introduction to Khiyar and the Proof for Khiyar al-Shart
Introduces types of khiyar and provides proof for khiyar al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three independent types of khiyar in Hanafi fiqh and state the textual proof for khiyar al-shart.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-shart is an exception to the general prohibition of conditional sales, established by a specific hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah opens the chapter on options (khiyar) in sales. The commentator explains why this chapter follows the binding sales chapter: a sale may be binding (lazim) or non-binding (ghayr lazim). Among the many types of khiyar, three are treated independently: khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation), khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight), and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). They are ordered according to their effect on the contract. The passage then provides the hadith of Habban ibn Munqidh, which serves as the primary proof for the validity of khiyar al-shart despite its apparent contradiction with the general rule against conditional sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three independent khiyar types
From the passage, list the three types: khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-ru'yah, and khiyar al-'ayb. Note the reason given for their order: shart prevents the beginning of the ruling, ru'yah prevents its completion, and 'ayb prevents its bindingness.
This order is based on the stage at which each option affects the contract.
2
Locate the dalil for khiyar al-shart
The passage cites a hadith from al-Mustadrak narrated by Ibn Umar concerning Habban ibn Munqidh. The Prophet (ﷺ) granted him a three-day option due to his mental weakness, despite the general prohibition of conditions in sales.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit action of the Prophet (ﷺ) in allowing the condition (option) shows that such a condition is valid and does not invalidate the sale. The imperative 'sell' (بع) indicates the contract is sound despite the stipulation.
This establishes khiyar al-shart as an exception to the prohibition of 'sale with a condition'.
4
Understand the relationship to qiyas
The passage states that khiyar al-shart is 'contrary to analogy' (khilaf al-qiyas). Normally, a conditional sale would be invalid due to uncertainty, but the hadith provides a specific ruling that overrides the general principle.
This is a standard Hanafi approach: a text from the Sunnah can create an exception to a qiyas-based rule.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
For example, a buyer stipulates a 3-day inspection period when purchasing a used car. According to this hadith, the contract is valid and the buyer may cancel within the period. This is the basis for khiyar al-shart in Hanafi fiqh.
The default maximum period is three days, but longer periods can be agreed by mutual consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الخيار
[افصل في خيار الشرط]
قوله : باب الخيار ؛ اعلم أنَّ البيع قد يكون لازماً، وهو ما لا يكون فيه خيار بعد وجود شرائطه، وقد يكون غير لازم، وهو ما فيه الخيار، ولما كان الأول أقوى في كونه بيعاً فقدمه المصنف له وأورد للثاني هذا الباب.
والخيار أنواعه وإن بلغت إلى سبعة عشر كما فصلها صاحب «الأشباه»، لكن الثلاثة منها، وهي: تورد بالاستقلال: خيارُ الشّرط والرؤية والعيب ؛ لكثرة أحكامها، وضخامة مسائلها فقدم المصنف ذكر خيار الشرط ؛ لأنه يمنع ابتداء الحكم، ثم خيار الرؤية ؛ لأنه يمنع تمام الحكم ، ثمّ خيار العيب ؛ لأنه يمنعُ لزوم الحكم، والخيار بمعنى الاختيار، قال في «المصباح المنير»: «الخيارُ : هو الاختيار، ومنه يقال له : خيار الرؤية». انتهى.
قوله : صح : اعلم أنَّ الخيار ثابت بالنص على خلاف القياس ؛ لأنه نهى عن بيع وشرط ، إلا أنَّ النص ورد به ، وهو ما أخرجه الحاكم في «المستدرك»: عن ابنِ عمره قال : «حبان بن منقذ له ، وهو ممن شهد أحداً، وكان رجلاً ضعيفاً قد سفع في رأسه مأمومة - أي ضرب في رأسه شجّة بلغت أمّ دماغه - ، فجعل له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الخيار فيما اشتراه ثلاثة أيام، وكان قد ثَقُلَ لسانه ، فقال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : بع ،
Chapter of Option. [Section on Option of Stipulation] His saying: 'Chapter of Option'; know that sale may be binding, which is that in which there is no option after the existence of its conditions, and may be non-binding, which is that in which there is the option. Since the first is stronger in being a sale, the author gave it precedence and presented this chapter for the second. The types of option, even if they reach seventeen as detailed by the author of al-Ashbah, but three of them are presented independently: the option of stipulation, the option of sight, and the option of defect; because of the abundance of their rulings and the magnitude of their issues. So the author gave precedence to the mention of the option of stipulation because it prevents the beginning of the ruling, then the option of sight because it prevents the completion of the ruling, then the option of defect because it prevents the bindingness of the ruling. And 'option' means choice. He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'Al-khiyar is choice, and from it is said: option of sight.' End. His saying: 'it is correct'; know that the option is established by text contrary to analogy, because he forbade a sale with a condition, but the text came with it, and it is what al-Hakim narrated in al-Mustadrak from Ibn Umar: 'Habban ibn Munqidh, and he was among those who witnessed Uhud, and was a weak man who had been struck in his head with a head wound reaching the membrane of his brain, so the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) gave him the option in what he bought for three days, and his tongue had become heavy, so the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said to him: sell...'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah, introducing the chapter on options (khiyar) in sales. The commentator first explains why this chapter comes after the chapter on sales: a sale may be binding (lazim) or non-binding (ghayr lazim) depending on the presence of an option. Since a binding sale is stronger, it was discussed first, and now the non-binding type is addressed. He then notes that although there are up to seventeen types of khiyar as listed in al-Ashbah, three are dealt with independently: khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation), khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight), and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). The order of presentation is deliberate: khiyar al-shart comes first because it prevents the initial effect of the contract; khiyar al-ru'yah follows because it prevents the completion of the contract; and khiyar al-'ayb comes last because it prevents the contract from becoming binding. The commentator then defines khiyar as 'choice' and cites al-Misbah al-Munir. Finally, he provides the textual proof for khiyar al-shart: the hadith of Habban ibn Munqidh, who was given the option of three days by the Prophet (ﷺ) due to his weak mental state. This establishes that khiyar al-shart is valid despite being contrary to qiyas (analogy), since the general rule forbids a sale with a condition that creates uncertainty.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The right to choose or cancel a contract; option.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of stipulation; a right to cancel the contract within a specified period agreed upon at the time of contract.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of sight; the right to cancel a contract if the purchased item is not seen before the contract.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect; the right to return a defective item if the defect existed at the time of sale and was unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author present Khiyar al-Shart before Khiyar al-Ru'yah?
A) Because it is more common
B) Because it prevents the beginning of the ruling
C) Because it has stronger proof
D) Because it is mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-shart is valid as an exception to the prohibition of conditional sales, proven by the hadith of Habban ibn Munqidh.

intermediate

Khayar al-Shart: Option for Both Parties with Three Days
Definition, proof, and dispute on option of stipulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on khayar al-shart, identify the opposing view of Sufyan al-Thawri, and evaluate the Hanafi response using the principle of common need (hajah).

Core Idea
Khayar al-shart is valid for both parties up to three days because the need to avoid deception is equal for buyer and seller.

This lesson examines the option of stipulation in Hanafi fiqh—a right granted by the Sacred Law to rescind or confirm a sale. The core ruling, that this option is available to both the buyer and seller for a period of three days, is established by the hadith of Habban ibn Munqidh. However, early jurists like Sufyan al-Thawri confined it to the buyer alone. We will explore the proofs and reasoning behind the Hanafi position and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the explicit ruling in the matn: 'Khayar al-shart is for each of the two contracting parties, and for them three days or less, not more.' This means both buyer and seller have the option.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Sufyan al-Thawri and Ibn Shubruma hold the option is only for the buyer, because the hadith only mentions a buyer being given the option.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand their reasoning: the option is established contrary to the general prohibition of combining sale with a condition, so it must be confined to the specific case of the hadith (the buyer). This is a strict application of the principle that exceptions are not to be extended.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the effective cause ('illa) for the option is the need to prevent deception by deliberation, which applies equally to both parties. Additionally, the wording in Ibn Majah's version ('idha ba'ata' – when you sell) includes both selling and buying, indicating the option applies to the seller as well.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if only the buyer has the option, a seller who is cheated cannot rescind the sale, causing hardship. The Hanafi position removes this hardship by allowing both parties to protect themselves.
