[1] قوله : بالخيار ؛ قيد به لأنه لو كان الخيار للبائع فَسَلَّمَ المبيع إلى المشتري فأودعه البائع، فهلك عنده بطل البيع عند الكل، ولو كان البيع باناً فقبض المشتري المبيع بإذن البائع أو بغير إذنه ثمَّ أودعه البائع ، فهلك كان على المشتري اتفاقاً ؛ لصحة الإيداع. كذا في «البحر» نقلا عن «التاتارخانية». [2] قوله : وعندهما ؛ حاصله : أنَّ الملك ما ثبت للمشتري كما هو مقرر عندهما فصح إيداعه ؛ لأنه متفرع على الملك ، فلم يرتفع القبض ؛ لأن ارتفاعه كان في عدم ثبوتِ الملك فهلاكه عند البائع كان بمنزلة هلاكه عند المشتري.
([The clause] 'because possession is nullified by return due to lack of ownership'): That is, the buyer with the option, if he takes possession of his purchase, then deposits it with the seller, and it perishes in the seller's hand, its destruction in his hand is upon the seller; because possession has been nullified by return, because the buyer did not own it, so the deposit was not valid. Rather, returning it to the seller is a nullification of possession, so the destruction occurs before possession, so it is upon the seller. According to the two companions, since the buyer owned it, his deposit is valid, so possession is not nullified, so it is as if it perished in the buyer's hand, so the destruction is from his wealth. (The phrase 'and the option remains of mā-dūn' – he bought something with an option, then either it perishes or he takes ownership – its meaning: the destruction of what the buyer purchased in the seller's hand is upon the seller, not upon the buyer. The scenario of its destruction in the seller's hand: the buyer took possession with the seller's permission and deposited it with him. Since the buyer is not an owner due to the option and is not a possessor due to the return, there is nothing upon him according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. [1] He said: 'with the option' – this is a qualification because if the option were for the seller, and he delivered the sold item to the buyer, then the seller deposited it with him, and it perished in his hand, the sale is void according to all. And if it were an absolute sale (without option) and the buyer took possession with or without the seller's permission, then deposited it with the seller, and it perished, it is upon the buyer by agreement, because the deposit is valid. As in al-Bahr, quoting al-Tatarkhaniyya. [2] He said: 'according to the two companions' – its summary: that ownership was not established for the buyer as is established according to them, so his deposit is valid because it is based on ownership, so possession is not nullified; because its nullification was in the absence of established ownership, so its destruction with the seller is like its destruction with the buyer.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the situation where a buyer purchases an item with an option (khiyar al-shart), takes possession, then deposits the item with the seller, and the item is destroyed while in the seller's custody. The core issue is whether the deposit is valid and whether possession (qabd) is considered to have been transferred back to the seller. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer does not fully own the item during the option period (since ownership is suspended), so his deposit is invalid. By returning the item to the seller, the buyer effectively nullifies his own possession, meaning the seller is deemed to still hold the item. Therefore, the destruction is treated as occurring before the buyer's possession, and the loss falls on the seller. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the buyer does own the item during the option period (ownership is immediate but subject to rescission), so his deposit is valid. Hence possession does not revert to the seller, and the destruction is considered to have happened in the buyer's possession, making the loss the buyer's. The passage then clarifies the phrase 'and the option remains of mā-dūn' – a Persian expression meaning 'either it perishes or I take ownership' – and reiterates the scenario. Two glosses follow: one distinguishing this case from when the option is for the seller or when the sale is absolute (bātan), and the second summarizing the reasoning of the two companions.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery of the purchased item.
إيداع (īdā‘): Deposit; placing an item in another's custody for safekeeping.
خيار (khiyār): Option or right to rescind a contract within a specified period.
مادون (mā-dūn): A Persian phrase meaning 'either it perishes or I take ownership' (یا هلاك می‌شوم یا مالك آن كردم). Used in the commentary to explain the outcome of the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Both equally
D) The depositary

Key Takeaway
The liability for loss when a buyer with khiyar deposits the item with the seller hinges on the Hanafi view of ownership — suspended (Abu Hanifa) or immediate (Sahibayn), leading to opposite conclusions.

Madhun's Khiyar After Price Waiver: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar effect after seller's ibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a madhun slave retains khiyar after the seller waives the price, and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The madhun's return after price waiver is either refusal to own (Abu Hanifa) or forbidden gratuitous transfer (Sahibayn).

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute involving a permitted slave (madhun) who buys an item with an option (khiyar). During the option period, the seller releases the slave from the price. Does the slave still have the right to return the item? The answer hinges on whether ownership is transferred at the moment of contract or upon option expiry. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, offer contrasting views, each supported by distinct analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key actors
A madhun slave purchases an item with a khiyar (option). During the option period, the seller waives the price (ibra'). Determine the legal question: does the slave retain the right to rescind?
The madhun is a slave permitted to trade; he has capacity for acquisition but not for gratuitous transfers.
2
State the core legal issue
The dispute centers on whether ownership of the item is transferred to the slave at the moment of the release. If ownership is established, returning the item would be a transfer without compensation (tabarru'), which the madhun cannot do. If ownership is not established (due to khiyar), returning is merely a refusal to accept ownership, which he can do.
This is a dispute over the legal effect of khiyar on ownership.
3
Outline Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa: the option remains. Because during khiyar, the slave does not own the item; ownership is suspended. Therefore, returning the item is a refusal to accept ownership (imtina'), not a transfer. The madhun has the authority to refuse a gift, as analogized from the case of a gift.
The key analogy: if someone gifts something to a madhun, he may refuse it. Here, accepting the price waiver is akin to accepting a gift; refusing the item is like refusing the gift.
4
Outline the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn): the option is lost. Because the release of the price causes the slave to own the item (ownership is established). Returning it would be a gratuitous transfer (tamlik bi-ghayr 'iwad), which a madhun is not competent to perform.
The Sahibayn further differ on the validity of the release itself: Abu Yusuf says the release is invalid (since the price is not yet owed), while Muhammad says it is valid by istihsan.
5
Note the sub-dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Within the Sahibayn, there is a difference on whether the release (ibra') is valid at all. Abu Yusuf holds it invalid because the price is not yet a debt (khiyar suspends the obligation). Muhammad holds it valid by istihsan because the cause of ownership (the contract) exists. However, both agree that if the release is valid, the option is lost.
This sub-dispute shows that the outcome can also depend on the validity of the release, not just its effect on ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَبْرَاهُ" بائعه عن ثمنه في المدَّة ؛ لأنَّ المأدُونَ يلي عدم التَّمَلكِ) : أي إن اشْتَرَى عبد مأذون شيئا بالخيار، وأبرأه بائعُهُ عن ثمنه في مُدَّةِ الخيارِ، بَقِي خِيارُهُ عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما " : لا يبقى له الخيار ؛ لأَنَّهُ إن بقي كان له ولايةُ الرَّد، فردُّهُ يكون تمليكاً بغيرِ عِوَض ، والمأذونُ لا يملك ذلك ، وعند أبي حنيفة ه لَمَّا لم يَمْلِكُهُ كان رَدُّهُ امتناعاً عن السَّمَلُكِ، وللمأذون ولاية ذلك ؛ فإِنَّهُ إذا وهب له شيئاً فله ولاية أنْ لا يَقْبَلَهُ. [۱]قوله : وأبرأه ؛ فإن قلت : إذا كان الخيار للمشتري فالثّمن لم يخرج عن ملكه ، فما وجه إبراء البائع عن الثمن قبل أن يملكه؟ أجيب : بأن القياس ينفي صحة هذا الإبراء، وجوازه استحسان ؛ لحصوله بعد وجود سبب الملك :، وهو العقد. كذا في «المنح». ('And the seller released him from the price within the period, because the permitted one [madhun] handles the refusal of ownership'): That is, if a permitted slave bought something with an option, and his seller released him from the price during the option period, his option remains according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions, his option does not remain; because if it remains, he would have the authority to return, and his return would be a transfer of ownership without compensation, and the permitted one does not have authority for that. According to Abu Hanifa, since he does not own it, his return is a refusal to take ownership, and the permitted one has authority for that; for if something is gifted to him, he has authority not to accept it. [1] He said: 'released him' – if you say: when the option is for the buyer, the price has not left his ownership, so what is the basis for the seller's release from the price before he owns it? The answer: analogy negates the validity of this release, but its permissibility is by istihsan, because it occurs after the existence of the cause of ownership, which is the contract. As in al-Minh. [2] He said: 'slave' – indicating that the intended 'madhun' is a permitted slave, not a minor or insane person. Permission is of two types: one: permission of the slave, which is removing the restriction due to slavery established legally upon the slave, and dropping the right, so he acts for himself with his original capacity. The second type: permission of the minor and insane, which is removing the restriction and establishing guardianship for them. As in al-Durar. [3] He said: 'according to the two companions' – that is, when his seller released him from the price, he does not retain the option; because when the permitted slave owned it, his return would be a grant without compensation, which is a gratuitous act, and the permitted slave is not competent for that, as established in its place. In al-Bahr he said: 'This requires the validity of the release, and we stated that it is not valid according to Abu Yusuf, and it is valid according to Muhammad by istihsan.' End. [4] He said: 'according to Abu Hanifa' – its summary: that since ownership is not established for the permitted slave due to the option, if he returns his purchase during the option period, his return is a refusal to take ownership, and he has authority for that.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a second intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a permitted slave (madhun) who purchases an item with a khiyar option, and during the option period, the seller waives (lit. releases from) the price. The question is whether the slave still retains his right to rescind the sale (khiyar). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the option remains because the slave does not own the item during khiyar (ownership is suspended). Therefore, if he returns the item, it is not a transfer of ownership (which he cannot do as a madhun) but merely a refusal to accept ownership, which he is permitted to do. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the option is lost because the slave, by the release, now owns the item (ownership is established), so returning it would be a gratuitous transfer (hiba) without compensation, which a madhun is not competent to do. The passage includes several glosses. The first gloss explains that the release of the price is valid by istihsan (juristic preference) even though analogically it would be invalid because the price is not yet owed. The second gloss defines 'madhun' as a slave permitted to trade, distinguishing it from permission for minors and the insane. The third gloss clarifies that according to Sahibayn, the option is lost, and it notes that within Sahibayn, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the validity of the release itself (Abu Yusuf says invalid, Muhammad says valid by istihsan). The fourth gloss restates Abu Hanifa's reasoning.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma’dhūn): A slave who has been granted permission (idhn) by his master to engage in trade and commercial transactions for his own benefit.
إبراء (ibrā’): Release or discharge from a debt; here, the seller waives the price owed by the buyer.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that overrides qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more equitable ruling.
خيار (khiyār): Option or right to rescind a contract within a specified period.
تمليك بغير عوض (tamlik bi-ghayr ‘iwad): Transfer of ownership without compensation; a gratuitous transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why does the madhun slave retain his option (khiyar) after the seller releases him from the price?
A) Because the slave owns the item and can do as he wishes
B) Because returning the item is a refusal to take ownership, which is allowed for a madhun
C) Because the release is invalid
D) Because the option is independent of ownership

Key Takeaway
The ruling on khiyar after price waiver depends on whether the madhun is deemed to have ownership during the option period: Abu Hanifa says no (option remains), Sahibayn says yes (option lost).

Khiyar of a Ma'dhun Slave After the Seller's Acquittance
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on khiyar of ma'dhun slave after seller's ibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi positions on the khiyar of a ma'dhun slave after ibra' and explain the rationale behind each position.

Core Idea
After the seller's ibra', Abu Hanifa keeps the khiyar; the Sahibayn extinguish it—a dispute over tabarru' capacity.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a ma'dhun (permitted) slave who purchases an item with an option (khiyar al-shart). When the seller acquits him from the price during the option period, what happens to the option? Abu Hanifa maintains the option continues, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) rule it falls. The disagreement turns on whether rejecting the sale constitutes a prohibited gift (tabarru') or a permissible abstention from ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the key facts: a ma'dhun slave buys something with khiyar al-shart; the seller acquits him from the price during the option period.
The slave does not own the item fully until the option period ends.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds the khiyar remains; the Sahibayn hold it is extinguished.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf within the Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
If the khiyar remained, rejecting the sale would mean returning ownership of the item without compensation. That is a gift (tabarru'), which a ma'dhun cannot do.
The key point: rejection = transfer of ownership without price.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Since the slave never fully owned the item during khiyar, rejection is merely abstaining from taking ownership—like refusing a gift. The ma'dhun has authority to refuse a gift.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between giving ownership and not accepting ownership.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the slave can still reject the sale after ibra'; under the Sahibayn, the sale becomes binding immediately.
This affects whether the slave must pay or can return the item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَبْرَاهُ بائعه عن ثمنه في المدَّة ؛ لأنَّ المأدُونَ يلى عدم التَّمَلكِ) : أي إن اشْتَرَى عبد مأذون شيئا بالخيار، وأبرأه بائعُهُ عن ثمنه في مُدَّةِ الخيارِ، بَقِي خِيارُهُ عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما " : لا يبقى له الخيار ؛ لأَنَّهُ إن بقي كان له ولايةُ الرَّد، فردُّهُ يكون تمليكاً بغيرِ عِوَض ، والمأذونُ لا يملك ذلك ، وعند أبي حنيفة ه لَمَّا لم يَمْلِكُهُ كان رَدُّهُ امتناعاً عن السَّمَلُكِ، وللمأذون ولاية ذلك ؛ فإِنَّهُ إذا وهب له شيئاً فله ولاية أنْ لا يَقْبَلَهُ.
And [if] the seller acquits him from the price within the period [of option]; because the permitted one [the slave] is in charge of non-ownership. That is: If a permitted slave buys something with an option, and his seller acquits him from the price during the option period, then his option remains according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], his option does not remain; because if it remained, he would have the authority of rejection, and his rejection would be a making of ownership without compensation, and the permitted slave does not have that [authority]. And according to Abu Hanifa, since he does not own it, his rejection is an abstention from ownership, and the permitted slave has authority over that; for if someone gives him a gift, he has the authority not to accept it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the khiyar (option of stipulation) of a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade) after the seller acquits him from the price during the option period. The base ruling is from the matn: if the seller acquits the slave from the price, what happens to the khiyar? The commentator expands: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave's khiyar remains; according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), it is extinguished. The reasoning for the Sahibayn: if the khiyar remained, the slave would have the right to reject the sale, but rejecting means returning ownership of the item to the seller without receiving anything in return—this constitutes a gift (tabarru'), which a ma'dhun slave is not permitted to do. Abu Hanifa responds: since the slave never fully owned the item during the khiyar period (ownership is suspended), his rejection is merely an abstention from taking ownership, which is like refusing a gift—something a ma'dhun is allowed to do. This distinction hinges on the nature of ownership during khiyar and the slave's capacity for tabarru'.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; his transactions are valid within limits, but he cannot make gifts (tabarru')
الإبراء (al-ibra'): The seller's act of releasing the buyer from the obligation to pay the price
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) allowing either party to rescind the sale within a specified period

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the slave becomes the owner of the item immediately
B) Because rejecting the sale would be a gift (tabarru'), which a ma'dhun cannot perform
C) Because the seller's acquittance is invalid
D) Because the option period ends with acquittance by default

Key Takeaway
The continuity of khiyar after ibra' depends on whether rejection is seen as tabarru' or mere abstention from ownership.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Wine Purchase with Khiyar upon Buyer's Conversion
Intra-Hanafi ruling on khiyar when dhimmi buyer of wine converts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a dhimmi buyer's conversion to Islam on a wine purchase subject to khiyar al-shart, and apply the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa voids the purchase to prevent Muslim ownership of wine; Sahibayn bind the sale and void the option to prevent Muslim facilitating wine ownership.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf concerning khiyar al-shart when the buyer of wine becomes Muslim. The ruling depends on whether the Muslim, after conversion, could potentially own wine or facilitate its ownership by another. The passage presents the views of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and emphasizes that these cases are practical consequences (thamarat al-khilaf) of a deeper disagreement about the nature of khiyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and contract
A dhimmi buys wine from another dhimmi with a khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation). The buyer then converts to Islam during the option period.
The contract involves wine, which is non-property (ghayr mal) for Muslims but permissible among dhimmis.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
According to Abu Hanifa, the purchase becomes void (batil) immediately upon conversion. His reasoning (dalil): if the option remained, the buyer could drop it and thereby own wine as a Muslim, which is prohibited.
The key concern is preventing a Muslim from owning wine.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the sale is executed and binding, and the option becomes void. Their reasoning: if the option remained, the Muslim buyer could return the wine, but returning entails transferring ownership of wine to the seller, and a Muslim cannot make another own wine. Therefore, the option is invalidated and the sale is upheld.
The focus is on the Muslim's incapacity to cause wine ownership.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference (thamarah) is that under Abu Hanifa, the buyer simply returns the wine; under the Sahibayn, the sale is final and the buyer must keep the wine (or dispose of it as a Muslim, e.g., pour it out).
The passage concludes that these are fruits of the dispute, not a single rule.

And the purchase of wine by a dhimmi from a dhimmi with an option becomes void if he [the buyer] converts to Islam; lest he own it as a Muslim by dropping his option. And according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], the purchase is executed and the option becomes void; because if it remained, he would have the right to return it, and returning would be a making of ownership, and a Muslim cannot make [another] own wine. These [cases] are fruits of the dispute.

Commentary
This passage discusses the case of a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected subject) who buys wine from another dhimmi with a khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation), and then the buyer converts to Islam during the option period. The ruling is stated: according to Abu Hanifa, the purchase becomes void (batil) immediately upon conversion, to prevent the Muslim from eventually owning wine if he drops the option. According to the Sahibayn, the purchase is executed and binding, and the option falls away; their reasoning is that if the option remained, the buyer (who is now Muslim) would have the right to return the wine, but returning would transfer ownership of wine to the seller, and a Muslim cannot facilitate ownership of wine by another. The commentator notes that these are practical consequences (thamarat al-khilaf) of the underlying dispute about the nature of khiyar and the capacity of a Muslim regarding wine. The text also references additional cases (footnote) that further illustrate the khilaf.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Muslim rule with a pact (dhimma) protecting his life and property
خمر (khamr): Wine; an intoxicating beverage whose sale is prohibited for Muslims but permissible for dhimmis among themselves
إسقاط الخيار (isqat al-khiyar): Dropping or waiving the option of stipulation, which consummates the sale

Key Takeaway
In intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, a dhimmi's conversion during a wine purchase with khiyar leads to two valid rulings: purchase void (Abu Hanifa) or sale binding with option void (Sahibayn).

Dhimmi Wine Purchase with Option: Dispute on Conversion to Islam
Ruling on dhimmi wine purchase with option and subsequent conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the voidness of a dhimmi's wine purchase with option upon his conversion, identify the Sahibayn's contrary view, and apply the principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
A Muslim cannot own wine, so Abu Hanifa voids the option sale; the Sahibayn void the option instead.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the intersection of sale (with option) and conversion to Islam. The matn declares the sale void if the buyer becomes Muslim, reasoning that otherwise he would own wine. The commentary contrasts the Sahibayn's view, who hold the sale valid but the option void. The passage then expands with further applications, making this a rich case study in talil (legal reasoning).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and rationale
Read the matn: the purchase is void if the dhimmi buyer becomes Muslim. The reason given is to prevent a Muslim from owning wine by dropping the option.
This is Abu Hanifa's position.
2
Recognize the Sahibayn's counterargument
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that if the option remained, the Muslim would have the right to return the wine, which is a transfer of ownership, and a Muslim cannot transfer wine ownership. Therefore the option must be void and the sale valid.
Their position avoids the transfer problem but creates ownership.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the sale is void (no transfer of wine to the Muslim). Under the Sahibayn, the sale is valid and the Muslim owns the wine (despite prohibition).
This is a key point of conflict.
4
Evaluate the additional masail
The commentary gives three more cases illustrating the same principle: fermented juice in a Muslim sale, staying in a purchased house, and a gazelle in ihram. For each, Abu Hanifa voids the sale to prevent unlawful ownership; the Sahibayn validate the sale due to inability to return the object.
These cases reinforce the underlying criterion.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Haskafi, Ibn Abidin) rely on Abu Hanifa's position as the operative ruling for fatwa.
The Sahibayn's view is noted but not adopted in practice.

And the purchase of wine by a dhimmi from a dhimmi with a condition of option becomes void if he (the buyer) becomes Muslim; so that he does not come to own it as a Muslim by dropping his option. (1) [1] (And the purchase...dropping his option): That is, if a dhimmi buys wine from a dhimmi with a condition of his option, then the buyer becomes Muslim, his purchase is void; because if it remained, then upon dropping the option the buyer would take ownership of it, thus a Muslim would come to own wine. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the purchase is valid and the option becomes void; because if it remained, he would have the right to return it, and returning it constitutes transfer of ownership, and a Muslim cannot transfer ownership of wine. So these issues are fruits of the disagreement. Refraining: for if someone gifts something to him, he has the ability to not accept, so here too. [2] [1] His saying: 'with a condition of his option'; he indicates that what is meant by option is the buyer's option. For if the option were for the seller and the buyer becomes Muslim, the sale is void, and if the buyer becomes Muslim it does not void, and the seller's option remains as it was. Then if he ratifies, the wine becomes owned by the buyer legally, and a Muslim is fit to own it legally. This is in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'these...etc.'; and some have added to what we have mentioned issues: 1. Among them: if the juice ferments during the period of a sale between two Muslims, the sale becomes void according to him (Abu Hanifa) due to inability to own it, and according to the two it is valid due to inability to return it. 2. And among them: if he buys a house on condition that he has the option while he is living in it by lease or loan, then continues living in it. Al-Sarakhsi says: it does not constitute a choice, and it is like starting residence. And Khwaharzada says: its continuation is a choice according to the two due to ownership of the property, and according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is not a choice. And among them: a halal person buys a gazelle with option, then takes possession, then enters ihram while the gazelle is in his hand: the sale is void according to him, and it is returned to the seller, and according to the two the buyer is bound (the sale is confirmed). If the option were for the seller, then it is void by consensus. If the option were for the buyer and the seller enters ihram, the buyer has the right to return it. : (1) As for if the seller becomes Muslim, it does not void by consensus and the purchase remains as it is. See: Majma' al-Anhur (2): (28). (2) al-Bahr al-Rai'iq (6: 17).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage opens with a ruling from the matn: 'وبطل شراء ذمي من ذمي خمراً بالخيار إن أسلم' meaning 'And the purchase of wine by a dhimmi from a dhimmi with a condition of option becomes void if he (the buyer) becomes Muslim.' The reasoning given in the matn is: 'لئلا يتملكها مسلماً بإسقاط خياره' i.e., to prevent him from coming to own it as a Muslim by dropping his option. The commentary then explains that if a dhimmi buys wine from another dhimmi with a condition of option, and then the buyer becomes Muslim, the purchase becomes void according to Abu Hanifa (عنده). Because if it remained valid, then upon dropping the option the buyer would take ownership, which would result in a Muslim owning wine, which is prohibited. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, عندهما), the sale is valid and the option is void. Their reasoning is that if the option remained, the buyer would have the right to return the wine, and returning it is a form of transferring ownership (تمليك), and a Muslim cannot transfer ownership of wine (since he cannot own it, he also cannot transfer it). Therefore, they hold that the option is void, and the sale is finalized. The passage then states that these issues are fruits of the disagreement (ثمرات الخلاف). Additional issues added by some scholars are then listed: (1) If wine (from grapes) ferments during the period of option in a sale between two Muslims, then according to Abu Hanifa the sale is void because the buyer cannot own it, while according to the two companions the sale is valid because the buyer cannot return it (since it's wine). (2) Regarding staying in a house that one has purchased with option: staying continuously is not considered a choice according to Abu Hanifa but is considered a choice according to the two companions. (3) If a Halal (lawful) person buys a gazelle with option, takes possession, and then enters ihram (state of pilgrimage sanctity) while the gazelle is in his hand: according to Abu Hanifa the purchase is void and the gazelle is returned to the seller; according to the two companions the buyer is bound (the sale is confirmed). If the option was for the seller and the buyer enters ihram, the sale is void by consensus. If the option was for the buyer and the seller enters ihram, the buyer may return it. The passage ends with a note that if the seller becomes Muslim, the sale does not become void by consensus, and the purchase remains as is (i.e., the buyer maintains his option).

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated condition in a sale allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state, protected under contract of dhimma.
خمر (khamr): Wine or intoxicating beverage derived from grapes.
إسقاط الخيار (isqat al-khiyar): Dropping or waiving the option right.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, voiding it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The sale is void and the option is void.
B) The sale is valid and the option becomes void.
C) The sale is valid and the option remains.
D) The sale is void and the option remains.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling on a dhimmi's wine purchase with option upon conversion turns on preventing Muslim ownership: Abu Hanifa voids the sale; the Sahibayn void the option.

Rescission Requires Notice: Hanafi Position on Verbal Faskh
Ruling on the effect of rescission without the other party's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that verbal rescission by the party with option does not take effect without the other party's knowledge, distinguish it from ratification and action-based rescission, and evaluate the opposing views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Verbal rescission binds only when the other party knows; ratification (ijaza) binds even without knowledge.

This passage from the chapter on khiyar al-shart addresses a critical procedural point: when a party with an option rescinds a sale, does the rescission take effect immediately or only upon the other party's knowledge? The Hanafi school, led by Imam Abu Hanifa, requires knowledge for verbal rescission to protect the innocent party from harm. Ratification, however, is effective without knowledge because it is a pure waiver of a right. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly implementing options in sales.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule on rescission and knowledge
From the text: 'لا يَنْفَسِخُ بلا علمه' – rescission does not take effect without the other party's knowledge. This is the core ruling for verbal faskh. Distinguish it from ratification (ijaza) which is effective without knowledge by consensus.
The rule applies to verbal rescission only; action-based rescission is effective without knowledge by agreement.
2
Identify the opposing views
Abu Yusuf (within Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i (outside) hold that rescission is effective immediately upon declaration, even without the other party's knowledge. Their reasoning: rescission is a unilateral act like divorce or manumission.
The text mentions 'خلافاً لأبي يوسفَ والشَّافِعِى'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Abu Hanifa argues that rescission affects the other party's rights and may cause harm. The other party may have relied on the contract (e.g., by transacting with the item). Requiring knowledge prevents such harm. The passage describes potential harm to the buyer if the seller secretly rescinds.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on preventing harm (darar).
4
Contrast with rescission by action
If the option-holder acts as an owner (e.g., selling the item, freeing a slave, having intercourse with a slave-girl), that constitutes constructive rescission and is effective without knowledge—this is agreed upon. The text gives examples in the commentary.
Rescission by action is an unambiguous indicator of intent to cancel.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, a secret verbal rescission is ineffective until communicated; under Abu Yusuf/Shafi'i, it is effective immediately. This affects liability for accessories (zawa'id) and risk of loss.
The passage mentions that accessories (zawa'id) follow ownership: according to Abu Hanifa, they go to seller; according to the two, to buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب الخيار - خيار الشرط -
ومن له الخيارُ يُجيرُ وإِن جَهلَ صاحبه ولا يَنْفَسِخُ بلا عليه

Book of Sales, Chapter of Option - Option of Stipulation - And whoever has the option may ratify even if the other party is ignorant, and it does not become void without his knowledge. And (1) 49 (And whoever has the option may ratify even if the other party is ignorant, and it does not become void without his knowledge): That is, if the one who has the option rescinds, it does not become void without the knowledge of the other party, contrary to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i. And among them: if the option is for the buyer and he rescinds the contract, the accessories are returned to the seller according to him (Abu Hanifa) because they did not arise in the buyer's ownership, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they go to the buyer because they arose in his ownership, as in al-Bahr, quoting from al-Fath and others. (r) 1] His saying: 'whoever has the option'—whether the seller, the buyer, or a third party. For the condition of option is valid from all of them. When he ratifies, it is valid, even if the other party does not know, by consensus, because it is a waiver of a right, so knowledge of the other party is not considered, like divorce and manumission. This is in al-Manh. But if the option is for both and one of them rescinds, the other may not ratify, because rescission cannot be followed by ratification, as in al-Durar. (0) ގ [2] His saying: 'if he rescinds'—by word; because if he rescinds by action, it is valid even without the other's knowledge by agreement. An example of rescission by action is that the seller, during the option period, acts in a manner of an owner, such as freeing the sold item, selling it, or if it is a slave-girl, having intercourse with her. This constitutes a constructive rescission because it is evidence of retentiveness. This is when the option is for the seller. As for when the option is for the buyer and he does these actions, the sale is confirmed. Even if the price is a specific item and the buyer disposes of it as an owner and he has the option, he thereby rescinds the contract. As stated in al-Inaya and others. [3] His saying: 'it does not become void without the knowledge of the other party'—because rescission is an act affecting the rights of the other party, and it is the lifting of the contract, and it is not free from harm. For when the option is for the seller, the buyer might have relied on the finality of the previous sale and transacted accordingly, and then the seller's rescission would cause him harm, such as liability for the value if the sold item perishes, and it may be... (1) See: al-Minhaj and its commentary Mughni al-Muhtaj (2:49) and others. (2) al-Bahr al-Rai'iq (6:17-18). (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:509). (4) Manh al-Ghaffar (Q2:11/a). (5) Durar al-Hukkam (2:153). (6) al-Inaya (5:512).
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage deals with the rule that the party who has the option (whether buyer, seller, or a third party) may ratify (ijaza) the sale, and it is valid even without the knowledge of the other party—this is by consensus. However, if he rescinds (faskh), the rescission does not take effect without the knowledge of the other party, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i. The reasoning is that rescission is an act that affects the rights of the other party, and it may cause harm. For example, if the seller has the option and rescinds without informing the buyer, the buyer may have already acted relying on the sale, incurring liability. The passage further distinguishes between rescission by word (which requires knowledge) and rescission by action (which does not require knowledge, by agreement). Examples of rescission by action include the seller disposing of the sold item as an owner (e.g., freeing a slave, selling it, or having intercourse with a slave-girl) during the option period. This constitutes a constructive rescission because it indicates the seller's intention to retain ownership. If the buyer has the option and does such acts, the sale is confirmed. The passage also discusses the fate of increase (zawa'id) during the option period when rescission occurs: according to Abu Hanifa, the increase goes to the seller; according to the two companions, it goes to the buyer. Then the commentary elaborates on the identity of the one with option, stating that any of them can exercise option, and ijaza is valid without knowledge because it is akin to relinquishing a right, similar to divorce or manumission. The text then details the reasoning why rescission requires knowledge: it is an act that burdens the other party and can cause harm, as the innocent party may have already transacted based on the assumption of the sale's finality.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): An option or right to choose to confirm or rescind a contract.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, voiding it.
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification or approval of a contract, confirming it.
إسقاط حق (isqat haqq): Waiver of a legal right.
ثمرات الخلاف (thamarat al-khilaf): The practical legal consequences that arise from a juristic disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a seller with an option secretly decides to rescind the sale without informing the buyer, what is the status of the rescission?
A) It is effective immediately and the sale is void.
B) It is not effective until the buyer learns of it.
C) It is effective only if the seller acts physically.
D) It is effective only if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
Verbal rescission under the Hanafi school requires the other party's knowledge to be effective, while ratification and action-based rescission do not.

Inheritance of Options in Sales
Stating which khiyar types are inheritable and which are not, with a note on the inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which khiyar types are inheritable in Hanafi fiqh and explain the reason for the dispute with al-Shafi'i and Malik.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, inheritable options are binding property rights; contingent personal options lapse upon death.

The inheritance of options (khiyar) in sales is a critical issue that affects the rights of heirs. The Hanafi school draws a clear line: options that are firmly attached to the contract of sale (like defect and specification) pass to heirs, while those that are personal and contingent (like stipulation and seeing) expire with the deceased. This lesson unpacks this distinction and examines why other schools, particularly the Shafi'i and Maliki, disagree regarding the option of stipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four khiyar types
From the matn, list the four options: khiyar al-'ayb (defect), khiyar al-ta'yin (specification), khiyar al-shart (stipulation), and khiyar al-ru'ya (seeing).
These are the standard options discussed in Hanafi sales law.
2
Determine which are inheritable per Hanafi
The matn states that khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ta'yin are inherited; khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya are not. Note the clear distinction.
The ruling is based on the nature of the right: binding vs. contingent.
3
Understand the Hanafi rationale
Khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ta'yin are considered binding rights (huquq lazimah) attached to the contract itself, so they transfer to heirs. Khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya are personal rights that expire upon death because they are contingent on the buyer's continued existence or optional choice.
The Hanafi view is that khiyar al-shart is a temporary suspensive condition, not a fully vested right.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i/Maliki position
Al-Shafi'i and Malik argue that khiyar al-shart is inheritable because it is a binding contractual right, analogous to khiyar al-'ayb. They see no essential difference in its binding nature.
This disagreement stems from different conceptions of the legal nature of khiyar al-shart.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a buyer who has a 3-day option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) and dies on day 2. In Hanafi law, the option lapses and the sale becomes binding on the heirs. In Shafi'i law, the heirs may exercise the remaining option.
This shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in estate outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويورَثُ خيار العيب والتَّعيين لا الشَّرط والرُّؤيةِ) : خيارُ التَّعيينِ : أن يشتري النوبين بعشرة على أن يُعَيِّنَ أَيَّا،شاء ، وخيارُ الشَّرط يورث عند الشَّافِعِي (٢) أَيْضاً [٤] قوله : يورث عند الشافعي ؛ لأنه حق لازم له في البيع فيجري فيه الإرث ؛ كخيار العيب، وبه قال مالك .
And the options of defect and specification are inherited, not the option of stipulation and seeing. Option of specification: e.g., he buys two garments for ten on the condition that he specifies whichever he wishes. The option of stipulation is also inherited according to al-Shafi'i. [4] His saying: 'It is inherited according to al-Shafi'i' because it is a right that is binding on him in the sale, so inheritance applies to it, like the option of defect; and Malik also said this.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on the inheritance of khiyar (options) in sales. The matn says: the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) and the option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin) are inheritable, meaning if the person who has these options dies, his heirs may exercise them. However, the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) and the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'ya) are not inheritable; they lapse upon death. The commentator defines khiyar al-ta'yin: e.g., buying two garments for ten with the condition that the buyer later chooses one. Then he notes a disagreement: al-Shafi'i holds that khiyar al-shart is also inheritable, because it is a binding right in the sale, like khiyar al-'ayb. Malik agrees with al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position is that it is not inheritable, because it is not a binding right but a contingent one that expires upon death. This ruling has practical implications for estate planning in sales.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the buyer's right to return a defective item.
خيار التعيين (khiyar al-ta'yin): Option of specification: right to choose from among multiple items within a specified period.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of stipulation: right to confirm or rescind within a stipulated period.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): Option of seeing: right to rescind if item is not as seen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following options is inheritable according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Khiyar al-shart
B) Khiyar al-ru'ya
C) Khiyar al-'ayb
D) Khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya

Key Takeaway
Only khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ta'yin are inherited in Hanafi fiqh; khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya lapse upon death.

Knowledge Condition and Inheritance of Options in Khiyar
Explains requirement of knowledge for rescission and inheritance of various khiyar types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the knowledge condition for rescission in khiyar al-shart and distinguish between inheritable and non-inheritable options.

Core Idea
Knowledge of rescission protects the other party; only options tied to property rights, not personal deliberation, are inherited.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses two critical issues in the Hanafi law of options: whether the other party's knowledge is required for rescission when a stipulation option (khiyar al-shart) is exercised, and which types of options are inheritable. The text presents the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the first issue, then clarifies that khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah are not inherited, while khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ta'yin are. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying khiyar in sales contracts and estate matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on knowledge condition
Read the opening statement: if the option-holder rescinds and the other party knows within the period, the rescission is effective; otherwise, the contract stands. Note that this is Abu Hanifa's position.
The ruling applies to verbal rescission only.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa's proof (qiyas)
The text draws an analogy to the dismissal of an agent (عزل الوكيل), which is contingent on the agent's knowledge to prevent harm. Identify how this applies to khiyar.
The harm to the seller is that he may rely on the contract's finality and lose market opportunities.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's counterargument
The Sahibayn argue that requiring knowledge defeats the purpose of the option if the other party hides or cannot be reached. Understand their reasoning.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi difference.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text and later authorities (e.g., Al-Marghinani) favor Abu Hanifa's view. State that the relied-upon position is that knowledge is required.
However, physical rescission (e.g., taking back the item) does not require knowledge.
5
Apply the inheritance rule to each option
List the four options mentioned: khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-ru'yah, khiyar al-'ayb, and khiyar al-ta'yin. Explain which are inherited and why.
Khiyar al-shart is not inherited because it is based on personal deliberation; khiyar al-ru'yah is not inherited because the sale of an unseen item is invalid in the stronger opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن فسخ وعَلِمَهُ في المدَّة انفسخَ وإلا تَمَّ عَقْدُه ويورَثُ خيار العيب والتَّعيين لا الشَّرط والرؤية) : خيارُ التَّعيينِ : أن يشتري النوبين بعشرة على أن يُعَيِّنَ أَيَّا ،شاء ، وخيارُ الشَّرط يورث عند الشَّافِعِي (٢) أَيْضاً القيمة أكثر من الثّمن، ولا خفاء في كونه ضرراً. . (۲) أحد وأما إذا كان للمشتري فالبائع عسى يعتمد تمامه فلا يطلب لسلعته مشترياً، وقد تفوت مدة أيام رواج بيع البيع، وفي ذلك ضررٌ لا يخفى، والتصرُّف المشتمل على ضررٍ في حق الغير يتوقف على علمه لا محالة، كما في عزل الوكيل ؛ فإنّه موقوف على علمه بعزل الموكل كي لا يتضرر بلزوم الثّمن فيما إذا كان وكيلاً في الشراء وببطلان قوله فيما إذا كان وكيلا بالبيع. [1] قوله : إنه ... الخ ؛ هذا معارضة على قولِ الطّرفين، حاصله : أنّ ما استدللتم به دل على كون العلم شرطاً للفسخ ، لكن عندنا ما يعارضه ؛ وهو أنه لو شُرِطَ العلم في الفسخ لم يبق فائدة الخيار. أو فائدتُهُ دفع الضرر عن نفس من له الخيار وإن اختفى صاحبه في مدة الخيار ولم يصل إليه خبرُ الفسخ فتم العقد ولم ينفسخ كما قلتم ، فيتضرر من له الخيار، وكان مجبوراً على دفع الضرر عن نفسه فما فائدة الخيار. [٢]قوله : انفسخ ؛ بحصولِ العلم به وإلا ؛ أي وإن لم يعلم به الآخر في المدة بل بعد مضي المدة ثم العقد ؛ لوجودِ الرّضاء دلالة حيث لم يتم الفسخ. (۳) قوله : يورث عند الشافعي ؛ لأنه حق لازم له في البيع فيجري فيه الإرث ؛ كخيار العيب، وبه قال مالك . (۱) محلُّ هذا الاختلاف في الفسخ بالقول، أما الفسخ بالفعل كالبيع والعتق وتوابعه والوطء ودواعيه بشهوة ضمني فلا خلاف في جوازه مع غيبة الآخر. ينظر: «الشرنبلالية» .(٢ : ١٥٣) (۲) ينظر: «تحفة المحتاج» (٤) : (٣٤٠) ، و(نهاية المحتاج»(٤ : ١١)، وغيرهما. كتاب البيع / باب الخيار - خيار الشرط - وخيار الرَّؤيةِ لا يَتَأَنَّى على مذهبه (۱) ؛ لأنّ شراءً ما لم يره لا يجوز عنده في أظهر القولين.
If he rescinds and the other party knows of it within the period, it is rescinded; otherwise, his contract is completed. And the option of defect and option of specification are inherited, not the option of stipulation and option of sight. Option of specification: that he buys one of two garments for ten on condition that he specifies whichever he wishes. And the option of stipulation is inherited according to al-Shafi'i as well... (commentary continues with arguments and counterarguments)
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the other party's knowledge is required for rescission in khiyar al-shart, and a ruling on inheritance of options. The matn states that if the party with the option rescinds and the other party learns of it within the period, the rescission is effective; otherwise, the contract stands. Then it states that khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-ta'yin (option of specification) are inherited, but khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) are not. The commentary explains the reasoning of both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the knowledge condition. Abu Hanifa's view is that knowledge is necessary to protect the other party from harm, as in the case of dismissal of an agent. The Sahibayn counter that requiring knowledge defeats the purpose of the option, as the option-holder may suffer if the other party hides. The commentary resolves that the dispute applies to verbal rescission only; physical acts like sale or manumission do not require knowledge. Regarding inheritance, the text notes that al-Shafi'i considers khiyar al-shart inheritable because it is a right that persists in the sale, analogous to khiyar al-'ayb. Al-Hanafi position is that it is not inherited because the purpose of khiyar al-shart is for the original party to deliberate, and that purpose expires with death. The passage also defines khiyar al-ta'yin and notes that khiyar al-ru'yah is not inheritable according to the Hanafi school because it is not established in the first place (since purchasing unseen is invalid in the stronger opinion).

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The right to rescind or confirm a sale within a specified period, stipulated in the contract.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The right to rescind a sale upon discovering a defect in the purchased item.
خيار التعيين (khiyar al-ta'yin): The right to choose one of multiple items purchased in a single contract within a fixed period.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The right to rescind a sale after seeing an item purchased unseen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following options is NOT inherited by the heir of the buyer?
A) Khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect)
B) Khiyar al-ta'yin (option of specification)
C) Khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation)
D) Both A and B are inherited

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-shart, the other party's knowledge is required for effective rescission (Abu Hanifa's view), and khiyar al-shart is not inheritable upon death.

Khiyar al-Shart Stipulated for a Third Party
Rulings on khiyar al-shart when the option is given to a non-contracting party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-shart stipulated for a third party and explain the reasoning behind its validity, including the preference for rescission when actions are simultaneous.

Core Idea
Stipulating khiyar for a third party is valid because the contracting party is treated as stipulating for himself with the third party as his proxy.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) is typically exercised by the contracting parties. However, a party may stipulate that a third party—an outsider—has the right to accept or rescind the sale. This lesson examines the validity of such a stipulation, the Hanafi proof based on avoiding nullity, and the priority rules when multiple parties act.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
The Hanafi school rules that khiyar al-shart stipulated for a third party is valid (sahih). The third party may accept or rescind, and both actions are effective.
The ruling applies to an outsider (ajnabi), not the other contracting party.
2
Identify the Proof
The proof is based on the principle that the contracting party's acts should not be rendered null (laghw) if possible. The stipulation for a third party is interpreted as stipulating for oneself and making the third party a proxy (na'ib).
This avoids invalidating the contract and gives effect to the contracting party's intent.
3
Note the Opposing View
Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) hold the stipulation invalid, arguing that khiyar is a personal right attached to the contract and cannot be delegated.
The disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response argues that the stipulation is made by the contracting party and can be viewed as an agency arrangement. Agency in khiyar is valid, so the third party acts as representative.
This is a qiyas jaliy: the stipulation for a third party is analogous to stipulating for oneself with an agent.
5
Determine Practical Consequences
Practically, the Hanafi position allows such stipulations, giving effect to the contracting party's intent. The thamarah al-khilaf is that under Zufar's view, the contract would be binding immediately, while Hanafi allows the third party to cancel.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.

وإن اشترى وشرط الخيار لغيره، فأي أجاز أو نقض صح ذلك، فإن أجاز أحدُهُما وفسخ الآخر، فالأَوَّلُ أَوْلَى ، وإن وُجدا معاً فالفسخُ أَوْلَى) ولنا : أن الغرض منه التأمل ؛ لغرض نفسه، وقد بطلت أهلية التأمل بخلاف خيار العيب ؛ لأن المورث استحق البيع سليماً ، فكذا الوارث ؛ لأنه ورث خياره ، وخيار التعين يثبت للوارث ابتداءً لاختلاط ملكه بملك الغير إلا أن يورث الخيار. [١] قوله : وان اشترى ؛ أحد شيئاً وشرط الخيار لغيره صح الشرط عندنا ، وقال زفر : لا يجوز؛ لأنه من أحكام العقد فيختص بالعاقد ، وبه قال الشافعي الله في قول. ولنا : أن تصرفات العاقد تصان عن اللغو مهما أمكن، فاشتراطه لغير العاقد اشتراط للعاقد، فيُجْعَلُ كأنه شرط الخيار لنفسه وجعل الأجنبي نائباً عن نفسيه اقتضاه تصحيحاً لتصرفه، والمراد بالغير في قوله : لغيره : الأجنبي، فلا يرد أنه صادق على العاقد الآخر ففسد المعني، فأي بمعنى له الخيار والأجنبي أجاز البيع أو نقض صح ذلك ؛ لأنّ كلاً منهما يملك التصرف أصالة أو نيابة. فإن أجاز أحدهما وفسخ الآخر: أي نقض الآخر، فالأول منهما أوفى ؛ لوجودو في زمان فيه لا يزاحمه فيه أحدٌ ، وتصرّفُ الآخر بعده لغو فيه. ولو وجدا : أي الإجازة والنقض معا : أي حال كونهما مجتمعين بأن أجاز أحدهما ونقض الآخر وخرج الكلامان معاً ، فالفسخُ أولى من أيهما كان، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف له ، وعن محمد تصرّف العاقد أولى لقوّته، وفقد ذلك أن تصرف النائب إنما يحتاج إليه عند انتفاء تصرّف المنوب ، وأما عند وجوده فلا احتياج إليه. واستشكل بما إذا وَكُل رجلاً بطلاقِ امرأته للسنة فطلقها الوكيل والموكل معاً، فإنَّ الواقع طلاق أحدهما لا بعينه.
And if he purchases and stipulates the option for someone else, then whichever accepts or rescinds, that is valid. If one of them accepts and the other rescinds, the first is preferred. And if they occur together, rescission is preferred. And for us: the purpose of it is reflection for his own sake, and the capacity for reflection has ceased, unlike the option of defect... (commentary continues)
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage addresses khiyar al-shart when the seller or buyer stipulates the option for a third party (not the contracting party). The matn rules: If the third party exercises the option (by approving or rescinding the sale), it is valid. If both the third party and the contracting party act—one accepting and the other rescinding—the one who acted first takes precedence. If both actions occur simultaneously, rescission is given priority. The commentary explains that this ruling is based on the principle that the contracting party's stipulation is interpreted as stipulating for himself, making the third party a proxy (na'ib). Thus, the third party acts as a representative. The commentary also cites the view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i that such a stipulation is invalid because the option is a right of the contracting party alone. The Hanafi response is that the stipulation is valid to give effect to the contracting party's intent. The commentary also discusses an analogy to agency in divorce (talaq) where if both the principal and agent pronounce divorce, the outcome is ambiguous but here rescission takes priority. The relied-upon position is that when actions occur together, rescission is stronger because it annuls the contract, and the original intent of the option is to provide an opportunity to withdraw.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): A contractual right to confirm or rescind a sale within a specified period.
الغير (al-ghayr): A third party who is not a party to the sale contract.
أجاز (ajaza): To approve or confirm the sale (thereby ending the option period).
نقض (naqada): To rescind or cancel the sale (فسخ).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In khiyar al-shart stipulated for a third party, if both the third party and the contracting party act simultaneously—one accepting and the other rescinding—what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The acceptance is preferred
B) The rescission is preferred
C) The contract is void
D) The first action in time prevails

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a khiyar al-shart can be validly stipulated for a third party, who may accept or rescind; simultaneous actions give priority to rescission.

Khiyar al-Shart for a Third Party: Valid by Deputyship
Ruling on khiyar al-shart for a third party with Zufar's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on khiyar al-shart for a third party, explain the niyaba (deputyship) argument, and identify the practical difference (thamarah) between the majority view and Zufar's view.

Core Idea
A condition of option for a third party is valid: the third party acts as the buyer's deputy.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi ruling on a sale where the buyer stipulates an option (khiyar al-shart) for someone outside the contract. The matn declares that such a condition is valid and the third party may confirm or cancel. The commentary then addresses Zufar's objection that the option belongs only to the contracting parties. The Hanafi response uses the concept of niyaba (deputyship): the buyer is treated as if he stipulated for himself and appointed the third party as his agent. This ensures the transaction is not rendered futile. We will walk through the dispute, the proof, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the matn: the condition of option for a third party is valid (sahih). The third party can cancel or confirm the sale. If both buyer and third party act together, cancellation takes precedence.
The matn also notes that khiyar al-ru'yah does not apply in this context per Abu Hanifa's more apparent view.
2
Identify Zufar's objection
Zufar (and al-Shafi'i in one narration) argue that khiyar is a ruling (hukm) of the contract and therefore belongs only to the contracting parties. A non-party cannot have such a right.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on avoiding futility (taghyīr al-laghw). The buyer's stipulation for a third party is interpreted as the buyer stipulating for himself and then making the third party his deputy (na'ib). This validates the condition.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under the majority Hanafi view, the third party can cancel or confirm. Under Zufar's view, the condition is invalid, so the sale stands without any option (unless there is another basis for khiyar).
5
Consider the simultaneous action case
The matn says if both buyer and third party act at the same time, cancellation is preferred. The commentary cites a narration from Abu Yusuf supporting this, and from Muhammad that the buyer's action is stronger. The Hanafi tarjih favors cancellation because a confirmed sale can be undone, but a cancelled sale cannot be reinstated.

(۱) ينظر: «الأم» (۳) :) (۳)، و«مختصر المزني : (۷۲) ، و فتوحات الوهاب»(٣: ٧٦) ، وغيرهما. زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية قالوا ذلك ; لأن شرط الخيار لغير العاقدِ إنَّما يثبت بطريق النيابة عن العاقد، فيثبت له اقتضاء. أقول : إذا اشترى على أن الغير بالخيار لا يثبت الخيار إلا برضاء المتعاقدين، فيكون نائباً عن المتعاقدين، ثمَّ رضاء البائع يخيار الغير لا يقتضي رضاه بخيار المشتري. وأجيب : بأن الترجيح محتاج إليه عند تنافي الفعلين كالفسخ والإجازة، وأما إذا اتحدا فالمطلوب حاصل بدونه فلا حاجة إليه. ، ووجه القول الأول : وهو الفسخ أولى : أنّ المجاز يلحقه الفسخ كمـا لـو أجاز والمبيع هالك عند البائع، والمفسوخ لا تلحقه الإجازة، فإنَّ العقد إذا انفسخ بهلاك المبيع عند البائع لا تلحقه الإجازة ولا خفاء في قوّة ما يطرأ على غيره على ما ليس كذلك. كذا في «المنح». Book of Sales / Chapter of Option - Option of Condition - And if he buys and stipulates the option for someone else, then whoever of them cancels or confirms, that is valid. If one of them confirms and the other cancels, the first is preferred. And if they occur together, cancellation is preferred. And the option of sight does not apply according to his madhhab, because buying what one has not seen is not permissible according to him in the more apparent of the two sayings. (And if he buys and stipulates the option for someone else, then whoever of them cancels or confirms, that is valid. If one of them confirms and the other cancels, the first is preferred. And if they occur together, cancellation is preferred.) And for us: the purpose of it is reflection for one's own interest, and the capacity for reflection has been invalidated, unlike the option of defect because the bequeather was entitled to a sound sale, so likewise the heir because he inherited his option. And the option of specification is established for the heir initially due to mixing of his property with another's property, unless the option is inherited. [1] His saying: 'And if he buys...' – one of them buys something and stipulates the option for someone else, the condition is valid according to us. Zufar said: It is not permissible because it is a consequence of the contract and thus specific to the contracting party, and al-Shafi'i said the same in one opinion. And for us: the actions of the contracting party are protected from futility as much as possible, so his stipulation for a non-contracting party is a stipulation for the contracting party, so it is treated as if he stipulated the option for himself and made the third party a deputy for himself, necessitated by correcting his transaction. And by 'someone else' in his saying 'for someone else' is meant a third party, so it is not objected that it applies to the other contracting party, thus corrupting the meaning. So whoever has the option – the third party – confirms the sale or cancels, that is valid because each of them has the right to dispose either directly or by deputyship. If one confirms and the other cancels, i.e., the other cancels, then the first of them is more effective because it occurs at a time when no one opposes him, and the action of the other after it is futile. And if they occur together, i.e., confirmation and cancellation together, meaning both are combined such that one confirms and the other cancels and both statements come out together, then cancellation is preferred from whichever of them it came. This is a narration from Abu Yusuf, and from Muhammad: the action of the contracting party is preferred due to its strength, and the loss of that means that the deputy's action is only needed when there is no action of the principal, but when present there is no need for it. And it is problematic with the case where a man appoints an agent to divorce his wife according to the sunnah, then the agent and the principal both divorce together – in that case, the actual divorce is from one of them not specified. (1) See: al-Umm (3:3), Mukhtasar al-Muzani (p.72), Fatuhat al-Wahhab (3:76) and others. Zubdat al-Nihaya li 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. They said that because the stipulation of option for a non-contracting party is only established by way of deputyship from the contracting party, so it is established by implication. I say: If he buys on the condition that the third party has the option, the option is not established except with the consent of the two contracting parties, so he becomes a deputy for the two contracting parties. Then the seller's consent to the third party's option does not necessitate his consent to the buyer's option. And it was answered: The preference is needed when the two actions conflict like cancellation and confirmation, but if they are unified, the desired result is achieved without it, so there is no need. And the basis of the first saying – that cancellation is preferred – is that what is confirmed can be affected by cancellation, as if he confirmed while the sold item was destroyed in the seller's possession, and what is cancelled cannot be affected by confirmation. For if the contract is dissolved due to the destruction of the sold item in the seller's possession, confirmation cannot affect it, and there is no doubt about the strength of what can supersede another versus what cannot. Thus in al-Manh. [1] His saying: 'it is established by implication' – there is discussion in it because the condition of implication is that the implied thing is of a lower rank than the implicator. Do you not see that if one says to his slave who has made an oath: 'Expiate your oath with wealth,' that is not an implied manumission because manumission is stronger than the action of expiation, as it is original, so it cannot be established as a branch of its derivative. And there is no doubt that the contracting party is of a higher rank, so how can the option be established for him by implication? And it was answered: Consideration is given to the purposes, and the third party is the intended one in stipulating the option, so he is the original with respect to the option, while the contracting party is original with respect to ownership not with respect to the option, so there is no necessity for the original to be established as a branch of its derivative. As for manumission, it is original in financial expiation, so it cannot be established as a branch of its derivative. Thus in Dhakhrirat al-'Uqba. (2) (2) His saying: 'I say' – this is a refutation of the establishment of the option for the buyer by implication. Its summary: The consent for this third party is not established unless the consent of the two contracting parties exists, so this third party is a deputy for both. And apparently, the seller's consent to his option does not necessitate his consent to the buyer's option, so the buyer's option is not established by implication. (1) Manh al-Ghaffar (q2:11/b). (2) Dhakhrirat al-'Uqba (p.358).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab (intra-Hanafi) dispute concerning the validity of stipulating an option (khiyar al-shart) for a third party in a sale contract. The author begins by stating the matn: if a buyer stipulates the option for someone else (a third party), that third party may either confirm or cancel the sale, and whichever action occurs first takes effect; if both occur simultaneously, cancellation is preferred. A side note mentions that the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) does not apply according to Abu Hanifa's madhhab because purchasing unseen goods is not permitted in the more apparent view. The commentary then focuses on the dispute: Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i in one narration hold that such a condition is invalid because the option is a consequence of the contract and thus belongs only to the contracting parties. The Hanafi response is that the buyer's actions are protected from futility; by stipulating for a third party, the buyer is effectively stipulating for himself and making the third party his deputy (na'ib). Therefore, the condition is valid, and the third party may act either directly or as a deputy. The text further discusses the case where both the buyer and the third party act simultaneously, with a narration from Abu Yusuf favoring cancellation and from Muhammad favoring the buyer's action. An objection is raised regarding how the option can be established for the buyer by implication (iqtida'), and it is answered by noting that the third party is the intended party concerning the option, while the buyer is original concerning ownership. The passage reflects the Hanafi commitment to validating transactions and avoiding nullity.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated right to cancel or confirm a sale within a specified period, as mentioned in the matn.
الغير (al-ghayr): A person not a party to the sale contract who is given the right of option by the buyer's stipulation.
النيابة (al-niyaba): Deputyship; acting on behalf of another. The third party acts as deputy for the buyer in exercising the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a buyer stipulates an option for a third party, the third party is considered a:
A) co-owner of the option
B) deputy (na'ib) of the buyer
C) deputy of the seller
D) independent agent of the contract

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a condition of option for a third party by treating the third party as the buyer's deputy (na'ib), thereby preventing the stipulation from being futile.

Khiyar by Requisition: When Intention Overrides Hierarchy
Critical refinement on khiyar established by requisition for non-contracting party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates khiyar for a non-contracting party via iqtiḍā' and evaluate the objection based on hierarchical rank.

Core Idea
Intentions reorder the hierarchy: the intended principal bypasses formal rank in iqtiḍā'.

You already know that a condition of option can be stipulated for a third party. But how does that option extend to the contracting party themselves? This passage tackles a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute over whether iqtiḍā' (requisition) can establish an option for the buyer when the condition names a third party. The resolution hinges on the critical role of intention (maqṣad) in defining legal hierarchies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the early position
Recognize that earlier Hanafis held that khiyar for a third party is established for the contracting party by way of iqtiḍā', because the third party acts as a deputy (nā'ib) of the contracting party.
This view is stated in the opening: 'They said that...'
2
Analyze the objection based on hierarchy
The critic argues that iqtiḍā' requires the subsidiary (far') to be of lower rank than the principal (aṣl). Since the contracting party is of higher rank than the third party, khiyar cannot be established for the buyer by iqtiḍā'. The analogy with manumission (that it cannot be established by iqtiḍā' from expiation) is invoked.
Look for the phrase 'فيه بحث' indicating a discussion.
3
Understand the response using maqāṣid
The respondent explains that in khiyar, the third party is the intended principal (maqṣad) for the option, while the contracting party is the owner of the subject matter. Thus, the hierarchy of options is reversed: the third party is aṣl regarding the option, so iqtiḍā' works.
The key phrase: 'الاعتبار للمقاصد' (consideration is for intentions).
4
Evaluate the tarjih (relied-upon view)
The later authorities (authors of al-Minaḥ and Dhakhīrat al-'Uqbā) side with the respondent, making intention the governing factor. Thus, khiyar for the contracting party via iqtiḍā' is valid when both parties consent.
This is the mufta bihi position.
